Talaq — Divorce

10 min
Conditional Divorce in Terminal Illness: The Firar Doctrine
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce in illness preserving inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for conditional divorce in terminal illness based on whose action triggers the condition, and apply the Hanafi position that preserves inheritance.

Core Idea
A husband's conditional divorce during terminal illness does not deprive the wife of inheritance if the condition is his action, her unavoidable action, or a third-party event also conditioned in illness.

A terminal husband might try to divorce his wife to prevent her from inheriting. The Hanafi school has a nuanced doctrine (firar) that protects her inheritance in many cases. This lesson unpacks the key scenarios: when the condition is the husband's own act, the wife's unavoidable act, or an external event, and whether the ta'liq was made in health or illness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of firar
Firar is the husband's evasion of inheritance by divorcing during terminal illness. The ruling aims to negate his intent.
The text mentions 'qasd al-ibtal' (intent to invalidate).
2
Identify the three categories of the condition
1) Husband's action (f'aluhu), 2) Wife's unavoidable action (f'aluha al-daruri), 3) Third-party action or time (ghayr al-ibnayn).
Each has a different ruling regarding inheritance.
3
Apply the timing distinction
If the ta'liq (attachment) was made in health and the condition occurs in illness, results vary. If both ta'liq and condition occur in illness, inheritance is usually preserved.
Exception: if the condition is wife's unavoidable action, she inherits even if ta'liq was in health.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
In the case of wife's unavoidable action with ta'liq in health, Muhammad and Zufar say she does not inherit; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say she does. The text adopts the latter.
The analogy to coercion (ikrah) supports attributing the action to the husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٤) قوله : لا ترث إجماعاً ؛ أي بيننا وبين الشافعي له وغيره، وذلك لانقطاع الزوجية مطلقاً عند موته، وهي من أسباب الإرث ، ويشترط في الإرث وجود سبب من أسباب الإرث عند موت المورث ، وبهذا احتج الشافعي له فيما قبل انقضاء العدة أيضاً. والفرق لنا : أنَّ العدّة يبقى فيها النكاح في بعض الأحكام ؛ ولهذا لا يحل له نكاح الأخت في عدّة الأخت ونكاحُ الخامسة في عدة الرابعة، فيجوز أن يحكم ببقاء النكاح في العدة في حق الإرث أيضاً، ويردّ ما قصده الزوج من إبطال حقها، بخلاف ما إذا انقضت العدة قبل موته ، فإنّه لا يمكن حينئذ بقاء حكم من أحكام النكاح، فلا يثبت الإرث أيضاً. [١] قوله : وإن ... الخ ؛ قال البِرْجَنْدي ؛ وإن علق بينونتها بشرط ووجـد ذلك الشرط في مرضه ترث إن علّق بفعله، الأحسن أن يقال : إن كان فعله ؛ أي إن كان الشرط فعل الزوج ، سواءً كان فعلاً له منه بد، أو لا، وسواء كان التعليق في الصحة ووجود الفعل في المرض، أو كانا جميعاً فيه ؛ لوجودِ قصد الإبطال فيه، بمباشرة الشرط. أو يفعلها، ولا بد لها منه، قيد في الأخير فقط ؛ أي علق بينونتها بفعلها الضروري، كالصلاة والصوم وكلام الأب وذوي الرحم المحرم، وطلب الحقوق من الخصوم، والأكل والشرب والقيام والقعود والتنفس، ونحو ذلك ففعلت في مرضه ترث ؛ لأنها مضطرة لخوف الهلاك في الدنيا أو العقبى، فكأنه ألجأها إلى المباشرة. أو بغيرهما وقد علق في المرض ؛ أي إذا علّق بينونتها بغير فعل الزوج والزوجة ، بل بفعل أجنبي أو بمجيء الوقت، وقد وقع التعليق والشرط كلاهما في المرض ترث أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ وقوع الطلاق عند وجودِ الشرط إنّما هو بمباشرة التعليق في حال تعلّق حقها بماله، فيكون فاراً، بخلاف ما إذا كان التعليق في الصحة، فإنّها لا ترث ؛ إذ عند التعليق لم يقصد إبطال حقها. [٢) قوله : بينونتها ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أن الفرار إنّما يتحقق في البائن دون الرجعي وإلى أن الواحد والثلاث وما بينهما سواء في الحكم.
In revocable divorce, she inherits in all circumstances. Her inheritance is specified to his death during her iddah. So they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) responded: "The action is unavoidable for her, so she is compelled to perform it, so her action becomes attributed to the husband as in coercion." By consensus: (And in revocable divorce she inherits in all circumstances) and her inheritance is specified to his death in her iddah: As for when her iddah expires, then he dies, she does not inherit. [1] His saying: 'So they responded' means the response from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and its summary: that his (the husband's) attachment (of the divorce) even if it was in health, but since he attached it to an action unavoidable for her, she is compelled to perform it in every state, so it is as if he caused the nullification of her right. [2] His saying: 'as in coercion' means that if Zayd coerces Amr to destroy another's property, and Amr destroys it, the guarantee is incumbent on Zayd, because the coerced becomes like a tool for the coercer, like a knife for the slaughterer, so the action is transferred to him. [3] His saying: 'in all circumstances' means whether the divorce was at her request or not, and whether the attachment was by her action or his action, and whether the action was avoidable or not; because revocable divorce does not completely remove the marriage, so that intercourse is lawful during the iddah, and he can take her back, so the cause of inheritance remains. This is in al-'Inaya. (4) His saying: 'she does not inherit by consensus' means between us and al-Shafi'i and others, because the marital relationship is absolutely severed at his death, and it is one of the causes of inheritance, and it is required for inheritance that a cause of inheritance exist at the death of the testator; and al-Shafi'i argued with this also regarding before the end of the iddah. Our distinction: that during the iddah the marriage remains in some rulings; therefore it is not permissible for him to marry the sister during the iddah of the sister, nor the fifth during the iddah of the fourth; so it is permissible to judge the continuation of the marriage during the iddah also regarding inheritance, and to nullify the husband's intent to invalidate her right; unlike if the iddah has expired before his death, then it is not possible for any ruling of marriage to remain, so inheritance is not established either. [1] His saying: 'And if... etc.'; al-Birjandi said: And if he attached her separation to a condition and that condition occurred in his illness, she inherits if he attached it to his action. It is better to say: if it was his action; i.e., if the condition was the husband's action, whether the action is avoidable or not, and whether the attachment was in health and the occurrence of the action in illness, or both were in it (illness); because the intent to invalidate exists, by directly performing the condition. Or to her action, and it is unavoidable for her, a condition only in the latter; i.e., he attached her separation to her necessary action, like prayer, fasting, speaking to the father and mahram relatives, demanding rights from opponents, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, breathing, and similar; so she does it in his illness, she inherits; because she is compelled for fear of destruction in this world or the Hereafter, so it is as if he compelled her to do it. Or to something other than the two and he attached in illness; i.e., if he attached her separation to something other than the action of the husband and wife, but to the action of a third party or the arrival of a time, and both attachment and condition occurred in the illness, she also inherits; because the occurrence of divorce upon the existence of the condition is only by the direct attachment in a state where her right is attached to his wealth, so he is a fugitive (from inheritance); unlike if the attachment was in health, then she does not inherit; because at the time of attachment he did not intend to invalidate her right. [2] His saying: 'her separation' contains an indication that evasion (firar) is only realized in a irrevocable divorce, not revocable, and that one, three, and what is between them are equal in the ruling.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, deals with the inheritance rights of a divorced woman in two scenarios: revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and conditional divorce during the husband's terminal illness (talaq al-marid). The text first establishes the general rule: in revocable divorce, the woman inherits from her ex-husband if he dies during her iddah (waiting period), regardless of the circumstances of the divorce. This is because revocable divorce does not completely dissolve the marriage; some effects, like inheritance, persist during the iddah. The commentary clarifies that this applies whether the divorce was at her request or not, and whether the condition triggering the divorce was action by her or him, avoidable or not. Then it addresses the case where the iddah has expired: she does not inherit, by consensus between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Shafi'i school argues that since marriage ends at death, there is no cause for inheritance; the Hanafi response is that during the iddah, the marriage partially persists, so inheritance is valid. The passage then delves into conditional divorce (ta'liq) made by a sick husband intending to deprive his wife of inheritance (firar). The commentary explains several sub-rulings based on the type of condition: if the condition is the husband's own action (whether avoidable or not), she inherits because he directly caused the divorce. If the condition is an action of the wife that is unavoidable (like eating, praying, or breathing), she inherits because she is compelled, and the divorce is attributed to him by analogy to coercion (ikrah). If the condition is neither the husband's nor the wife's action (e.g., an event or a third party's act), and the ta'liq was made during illness, she inherits; if the ta'liq was made in health, she does not. The key principle is that the husband's intention to evade inheritance (firar) is considered, and inheritance rights are protected. The passage also notes that this ruling applies only to irrevocable divorce (bain), not revocable, and that one, three, or any number of divorces are treated equally.

Key Terms
فِرَار (firar): Evasion of inheritance by a husband who divorces his wife during terminal illness to prevent her from inheriting.
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage completely, after which the husband cannot take her back without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional divorce during illness, which of the following scenarios does NOT result in the wife inheriting according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) The condition is the husband's own action, and the ta'liq was in health.
B) The condition is the wife's unavoidable action, and the ta'liq was in health.
C) The condition is the arrival of a specific time, and both ta'liq and condition occurred in illness.
D) The condition is the wife's unavoidable action, and the ta'liq was in health, but the action is avoidable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi firar doctrine protects the wife's inheritance when the husband conditionally divorces her during terminal illness, with details depending on the type of condition and timing.

Conditional Divorce in Terminal Illness: Protecting the Wife's Inheritance
Explaining rulings of conditional divorce during sickness affecting wife's inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of conditional divorce during terminal illness and determine whether the wife inherits in each scenario.

Core Idea
A husband cannot use a conditional divorce in his terminal illness to disinherit his wife; she inherits in most cases.

This lesson from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce by a terminally ill husband. The key principle is that the law blocks the husband's evasion (firar) of the wife's inheritance right. The passage categorizes conditions based on whose action triggers the divorce and whether the wife has an alternative. Understanding these categories is essential for applying the correct inheritance ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of conditions.
The text divides conditions into: (a) husband's action, (b) wife's necessary action (lā budda lahā minhu), (c) other actions (third party or time).
2
Understand the effect of timing of conditioning.
Whether the conditioning was made in health or illness matters for category (c) and for the ikhtilaf in (b). In health, the wife inherits only if the condition is the husband's act; for (c) she does not inherit.
3
Apply the rule of evading (firar) intent.
The wife inherits when the husband's conditioning aims to nullify her right, which is presumed when the condition occurs in his illness.
4
Note the distinction between bā'in and raj'ī divorce.
The ruling applies only to irrevocable divorce (bā'in), as the husband's intent to deprive inheritance is only possible when the divorce is final.

Source Text

[١] قوله : وإن ... الخ ؛ قال البِرْجَنْدي ؛ وإن علق بينونتها بشرط ووجـد ذلك الشرط في مرضه ترث إن علّق بفعله، الأحسن أن يقال : إن كان فعله ؛ أي إن كان الشرط فعل الزوج ، سواءً كان فعلاً له منه بد، أو لا، وسواء كان التعليق في الصحة ووجود الفعل في المرض، أو كانا جميعاً فيه ؛ لوجودِ قصد الإبطال فيه، بمباشرة الشرط.

[٢] قوله : بينونتها ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أن الفرار إنّما يتحقق في البائن دون الرجعي وإلى أن الواحد والثلاث وما بينهما سواء في الحكم.
(۱) انتهى من «النقاية»(ص۹۲).
Book of Divorce: Divorce of the Sick Person. The expression of 'al-Mukhtasar' is as follows: And if he conditions her separation upon a condition, and it occurs in his illness, she inherits if he conditioned it upon his own action, or upon her action and there is no alternative for her from it, or upon something else while having conditioned it in illness. So the summary is that if the conditioning is by his action, she inherits absolutely. If it is by her action and she has no alternative, then similarly, except that if the conditioning was in health, then there is a disagreement between Muhammad and Zufar. If she has an alternative, she does not inherit. And if he conditions upon other than their action, then if the conditioning is in illness, she inherits; otherwise not. The period of 'iddah also affects inheritance. And it defeats the husband's intent to nullify her right. Contrary to the case where the 'iddah expires before his death, for then no ruling of marriage remains, so inheritance is not established. [1] His statement: 'And if...' etc. al-Birjandi said: And if he conditions her separation upon a condition and that condition occurs in his illness, she inherits if he conditioned it upon his action. It is better to say: if it was his action, i.e. the condition was the husband's action, whether it is an action from which he has an alternative or not, and whether the conditioning was in health and the occurrence in illness, or both were in illness, because the intent to nullify her right exists by his direct performance of the condition. Or by her action and she has no alternative from it; restricted to the latter only, i.e. he conditioned her separation upon her necessary action, such as prayer, fasting, speaking to her father and unmarriageable relatives, claiming rights from opponents, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, breathing, and the like. So if she does it in his illness, she inherits, because she is compelled by fear of ruin in this world or the Hereafter; it is as if he forced her to perform it. Or by other than both and he conditioned in illness: i.e. if he conditions her separation upon other than the husband's and wife's action, such as a stranger's action or the coming of a time, and both the conditioning and the condition occur in illness, she also inherits, because the occurrence of divorce upon the condition's fulfilment is by direct conditioning while her right to his property is attached, so he is a defrauder. Contrary to if the conditioning was in health, then she does not inherit, because at the time of conditioning he did not intend to nullify her right. [2] His statement: 'her separation' indicates that evasion is realized only in irrevocable divorce, not revocable, and that one, three, and what is between them are equal in ruling. (1) End of 'an-Niqaya' (p. 92).
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the rulings of conditional divorce by a terminally ill husband (marad al-mawt). The core issue is that the husband may attempt to deprive his wife of inheritance by making divorce conditional on an event that will occur during his illness, thereby causing the divorce to take effect before his death, which would prevent her from inheriting. The Sharh clarifies that the wife inherits in most scenarios when the condition occurs during the illness, because the law blocks the husband's evasion (firar). Three categories of conditions are discussed: (1) Conditions that are the husband's own act: she inherits regardless, whether the conditioning was in health or illness, because the husband directly brings about the condition. (2) Conditions that are the wife's necessary act (lā budda lahā minhu) such as acts of worship or basic human actions: she also inherits, because she is compelled and has no choice. However, if the conditioning was in health, there is a disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar (likely about whether she inherits if the condition occurs in illness after a healthy conditioning). (3) Conditions that involve a third party or a time: she inherits only if both the conditioning and the condition occur during the illness. The passage also specifies that these rules apply to irrevocable divorce (bā'in), not revocable (raj'ī), and that the number of divorces (one, two, or three) does not affect the inheritance ruling. Finally, if the 'iddah expires before the husband's death, inheritance is not established because the marital tie is completely severed.

Key Terms
بينونتها (baynūnatuhā): Her separation from the husband; the state of being irrevocably divorced (bā'in).
البائن (al-bā'in): Irrevocable divorce, where the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage.
الرجعي (al-raj'ī): Revocable divorce, where the husband may reconcile during the 'iddah.
الفرار (al-firār): Evasion; the husband's attempt to deprive his wife of inheritance through a strategic conditional divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband in terminal illness says, 'If you pray Fajr, you are divorced,' and she prays Fajr during his illness, does she inherit from him?
A) Yes, because the condition is her necessary act.
B) No, because she chose to pray.
C) Yes, but only if the conditioning was also in illness.
D) No, because prayer is not related to divorce.

Key Takeaway
The wife inherits when a conditional divorce occurs in the husband's terminal illness, unless the condition is based on a non-necessary act of the wife and conditioning was in health, or the 'iddah expires before his death.

The Ruling and Definition of Revocation (Ruju') After Divorce
Introducing the definition and rulings of revoking a revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ruju', identify its conditions, and apply the ruling to scenarios of revocable and irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
Revocation is the continuation of marriage, not a new contract, and is valid only during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya introduces the rules of revocation (ruju'). The author defines it as the continuation of marital authority, distinguishes between the 'iddah of consummation and valid seclusion, and clarifies that the husband may revoke even without the wife's consent. The Quranic evidence establishes the right and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is revocation (ruju'). Determine its legal status: sunnah (recommended) as it is a right given to the husband to reconcile, but not obligatory.
The Hanafi position is that ruju' is sunnah, based on the Quranic phrase 'if they desire reconciliation'.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is Quran 2:228, which states that husbands have more right to take back their wives during the 'iddah.
This verse is the foundational proof for the validity of ruju'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ' explicitly gives the husband the right of revocation. The imperative and permissive style indicate it is a valid legal act.
The dalala is qat'i (definitive) in establishing the right.
4
Note the conditions
Ruju' is valid only during the 'iddah of actual consummation (dukhul), not valid seclusion (khalwa). It applies only to revocable divorces (first or second for free women; first for slave women). The wife's consent is not required.
The commentary explicitly excludes 'iddah of khalwa, even though it triggers 'iddah.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab difference
All schools agree on the basic ruling, but the Hanafi definition as 'continuation of marriage' rather than 'renewal' has implications for related rules like zihar and ila'.
The author argues that if ruju' were a fresh contract, those ancillary rules would not apply.

Source Text

هي في العدة لا بعدها لمن طلقت دون ثلاث، وإن أبت
باب الرجعة
(هي في العدة لا بعدها لمن طلقت دون ثلاث) : أي في الحرة ، أما في الأمة فلا رجعة إلا في الواحدة، وإن أبت (0)

Chapter on Revocation. It is during the 'iddah, not after it, for one who has been divorced less than three times, even if she refuses. Chapter on Revocation. (It is during the 'iddah, not after it, for one who has been divorced less than three times): i.e., for the free woman. As for the slave woman, there is no revocation except in the (first) one. Even if she refuses. [0] [1] His statement: 'Chapter on Revocation'; he mentioned it after divorce because it naturally follows it. 'Ar-raj'a' with fatha on the ra', and kasra also occurs - linguistically: return. Its verb is used transitively by itself, and intransitively with 'ila'. It is said 'raja'a ila ahlihi' (he returned to his family) and 'raja'tuhu ilayhim' (I returned him to them). Its verbal nouns also include 'raj'an', 'ruju'an', 'marja'an', 'raj'a', and 'ruj'a' with kasra. This is from 'al-Fath'. The jurists define it as: the continuation of marital authority existing in one sense, and evidence for it is His saying: 'And divorced women shall wait for three periods, and it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs, if they believe in Allah and the Last Day. And their husbands have more right to take them back in that period if they wish for reconciliation.' (2:228) Therefore, zihar and ila are valid from a woman in 'iddah, and she is included in his statement: 'My wives are divorced.' If the marital authority were entirely severed and revocation were a renewal, it would not be so. [2] His statement: 'During the 'iddah' means the 'iddah of consummation, not the 'iddah of valid seclusion (khalwa). For valid seclusion, even though it is like intercourse in requiring 'iddah, revocation is not valid therein. This is what Ibn Kamal mentioned in 'al-Islah'. [3] His statement: 'Less than three' because of His statement: 'Divorce is twice, then retention with honor or release with kindness' (2:229), and His statement: 'If he divorces her (i.e., a third time), then she is not lawful for him afterwards until she marries another husband' (2:230). [4] His statement: 'Except in the one' because of what was mentioned earlier that two divorces for her (slave woman) are like three for the free woman. [01] His statement: 'Even if she refuses'; the waw is conjunctive, i.e., even if the woman refuses and is not pleased with the revocation, for the woman's consent is not a condition in revocation, just as it is not a condition in divorce, due to the general import of the texts.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya opens the chapter on revocation (ruju'). The author explains that ruju' is the husband's right to take back his wife during her 'iddah after a revocable divorce (first or second divorce). It applies only to free women divorced fewer than three times; for a slave woman, ruju' is allowed only after the first divorce (since two divorces for her are like three for a free woman). The husband may revoke even if the wife refuses; her consent is not required, similar to the case of divorce itself. The legal definition of ruju' is 'the continuation of marital authority existing in one sense', supported by Quran 2:228, which states that husbands have more right to take back their wives during the 'iddah if they desire reconciliation. This definition explains why zihar (unlawful comparison) and ila' (oath of abstention) are valid from a woman in 'iddah, and why a general statement like 'my wives are divorced' includes her—if the marriage were completely ended, these rules would not apply. The commentary also specifies that the 'iddah referred to is that of actual consummation (dukhul), not that of valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha); even though khalwa triggers 'iddah, it does not allow ruju'. Finally, the proof for the limit of less than three divorces is given from Quran 2:229 and 2:230, which state that divorce is twice (allowing retention) and after a third divorce, the woman is not lawful until she marries another.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): Revocation: the husband's taking back his wife during her 'iddah after a revocable divorce, thereby continuing the marriage without a new contract.
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying. For a divorced free woman, it is three menstrual cycles (qurū').
الخلوة الصحيحة (al-khalwa al-sahīha): Valid seclusion: when a couple are alone together without impediment, legally equivalent to consummation for some rulings but not for ruju'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for which type of 'iddah is revocation (ruju') valid?
A) 'Iddah of valid seclusion (khalwa)
B) 'Iddah of consummation (dukhul)
C) Both types of 'iddah
D) Neither type of 'iddah

Key Takeaway
Revocation (ruju') is the husband's unilateral right during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce, and it is effective even without the wife's consent.

Definition and Conditions of Raj'a (Revocation)
Definition, conditions, and methods of raj'a with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define raj'a, identify its conditions, and distinguish valid methods of effecting raj'a according to the Hanafi school, including the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Raj'a is a continuation of marriage, not a new contract, so actions with desire suffice.

In this passage from the chapter on raj'a, we explore its definition as a continuation of marriage ownership during the 'idda, the conditions under which it is valid, and the methods by which it may be effected — whether by explicit words or by actions indicating desire. We also encounter a key intra-madhhab difference: the Hanafi view that actions alone can constitute raj'a versus the Shafi'i requirement of speech. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the law of revocation after a revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of raj'a
Carefully read the linguistic and technical definitions. Note that Hanafis define raj'a as a continuation (istidama) of existing ownership, not a new initiation. This conceptual foundation shapes all subsequent rulings.
The Quranic verse 2:228 is cited to support this understanding.
2
Determine the conditions for valid raj'a
Extract the conditions: must occur during the 'idda after actual intercourse (not seclusion only), for a free woman after less than three divorces (or one for a slave woman), and the wife's consent is not required.
The 'idda of seclusion (khalwa) does not permit raj'a.
3
Classify the methods of effecting raj'a
List the acceptable methods: explicit words (e.g., 'I take you back'), intercourse, touching with desire, looking at the private part with desire. Note the condition 'with desire' for actions, and that looking at other parts or without desire is insufficient.
Words are explicit and require no intention; actions are valid but disliked (makruh) compared to speech.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Examine the opposing view: that raj'a is akin to initiating marriage and therefore requires a verbal contract. Understand the Hanafi response that raj'a is a unilateral right for the husband's benefit, as a continuation of ownership.
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, intercourse with desire during 'idda counts as raj'a; in Shafi'i it does not.
5
Synthesize the practical application
Consider a scenario: a husband divorces his wife once revocably, then during the 'idda touches her with desire. According to Hanafi, this constitutes raj'a, so the marriage continues without a new contract. According to Shafi'i, it does not, and explicit words are needed.
This ruling applies only to revocable divorces (first or second for free women).

It is during the waiting period, not after it, for one who has been divorced less than three times, even if she refuses.
Chapter of Raj'a
(It is during the waiting period, not after it, for one who has been divorced less than three times): i.e., for a free woman; as for a slave woman, there is no raj'a except in the one [divorce], even if she refuses.

[1] His saying: Chapter of Raj'a; he mentioned it after divorce because it naturally follows it. Raj'a (with fatha on the ra, and kasra also occurs) linguistically means return. Its verb is used transitively and intransitively with 'ila'. It is said: he returned to his family, and I returned him to them. Its verbal nouns include raj'an, ruju'an, marji'an, raj'atan, and ruj'a (with kasra) as in al-Fath. The jurists define it as the continuation of existing ownership in some respect, and this is indicated by His saying: 'And divorced women shall wait for three menstrual periods, and it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs if they believe in Allah and the Last Day. And their husbands have more right to take them back in that [period] if they desire reconciliation' (2:228). For this reason, zihar and ila' are valid from the woman in 'idda, and she enters under his saying 'my wives are divorced', whereas if ownership had been completely terminated and raj'a were a reestablishment, it would not be so.
[2] His saying: during the waiting period; i.e., the waiting period of intercourse, not the waiting period of seclusion. For although valid seclusion is considered like intercourse in requiring 'idda, it is not valid to revoke during it. Thus stated Ibn Kamal in al-Islah.
(3) His saying: less than three; because of His saying: 'Divorce is twice, then holding in honor or releasing with kindness' (2:229), and His saying: 'And if he divorces her [for the third time], she is not lawful to him after that until she marries another husband' (2:230). [4] His saying: except in the one; because previously mentioned that two [divorces] for her are like three for a free woman. [ ] His saying: even if she refuses; the waw is conjunctive: i.e., even if the woman refuses and does not consent to raj'a, for the woman's consent is not a condition for raj'a, just as it is not a condition for divorce, due to the general import of the texts of the Book and the Sunnah.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'a
by something like 'I take you back' and by intercourse, touching with desire, and looking at her private part with desire. This is according to us. As for al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except by speech.
[1] His saying: by something like; meaning any expression indicating return, such as 'I take you back,' 'I retain you,' 'I hold you,' 'I return you,' 'I keep you,' and so on. All of this does not require intention because it is explicit.
[2] His saying: and by intercourse; i.e., these are the forms of raj'a by action. But they are not free from dislike, for the recommended is raj'a by speech, as in al-Fath.
[3] His saying: with desire; the condition of desire is considered in touching, and likewise in looking, and looking is restricted to her private part, so that if he touches her or kisses her or looks at her private part without desire, or looks at other than her private part with desire, it is not raj'a; because the act being raj'a is only because it is evidence, and evidence is only what is specific to it. Looking at the private part with desire, touching with desire, and kissing with desire are acts specific to marriage, especially for a free woman. Unlike touching and looking at the private part without desire, because that may be permissible without marriage, as in the case of a midwife, doctor, circumciser, and witness in adultery. And looking at other than the private part may occur between those living in a house. Thus in al-Hidaya and al-Nihaya.
[4] His saying: except by speech; i.e., if he is able to speak. If he is mute or has a speech impediment, his raj'a is valid by gesture. The reasoning is that raj'a is like initiating marriage, so it is not valid by action. But we say: the right of raj'a is established by law as a consideration for the husband, so that he may rectify when he regrets separation. This requires that he have independent authority over it; therefore raj'a is valid without her consent by agreement. And this indicates that raj'a is a continuation of existing ownership, not a complete initiation.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on raj'a (revocation of divorce) explains the definition, conditions, and methods of performing raj'a according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by defining the term linguistically (return) and technically (continuation of existing marriage ownership from one aspect). He cites Quran 2:228 as proof that husbands have more right to take back their wives during the 'idda, which indicates that marriage ownership persists. He then states that raj'a is only valid during the 'idda period resulting from actual intercourse, not from valid seclusion (khalwa). Furthermore, it applies only to divorces less than three (for a free woman) or one (for a slave woman). The consent of the wife is not required, as established by general scriptural proofs.

The passage then details the methods of effecting raj'a: explicit words like 'I take you back' (which do not require intention), or actions such as intercourse, touching with desire, or looking at the private part with desire. These actions must be done with sexual desire to be deemed indicative of marital intimacy. Without desire, they are not specific to marriage and thus do not constitute raj'a. The author contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, which holds that raj'a is only valid by speech, not by action. The Hanafi response is that the right of raj'a is established for the husband's benefit, allowing him to remedy regret, so he should be able to exercise it independently; this right is a continuation of existing ownership, not a new contract. If the husband is mute, gesture suffices. The recommended method is verbal, while action is permissible but disliked.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): Linguistically meaning return; technically, continuation of existing marriage ownership during the waiting period after a revocable divorce.
العدة (al-'idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying. Here refers to the 'idda of divorce.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire. Required for touching, kissing, looking to constitute raj'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions does NOT constitute a valid raj'a?
A) The husband says 'I take you back' during the 'idda.
B) The husband has intercourse with his wife during the 'idda with desire.
C) The husband looks at his wife's face with desire during the 'idda.
D) The husband touches his wife's private part with desire during the 'idda.

Key Takeaway
Raj'a is a unilateral act of the husband during 'idda after a revocable divorce, effected by explicit words or acts of intimacy with desire, without requiring the wife's consent.

Means of Revocation: Verbal and by Action
Explains conditions for verbal and physical acts to constitute valid revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal and physical acts constitute valid revocation (رجعة) in the Hanafi school and state the conditions of desire and specificity.

Core Idea
Revocation is valid by explicit speech or by acts specific to marriage done with desire.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the means of revocation in the Hanafi school. Unlike the Shafi'i school which requires verbal declaration, the Hanafis recognize certain physical acts as sufficient indicators of the husband's intent to resume the marriage. The key is that these acts must be done with desire and be specific to marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the means of revocation
List the verbal and physical acts mentioned in the text that constitute valid revocation: explicit speech (e.g., 'I revoke you'), intercourse, touching with desire, looking at private parts with desire, kissing with desire.
Verbal acts are explicit and do not require intention.
2
Apply the condition of desire
For physical acts (touching, looking, kissing), determine whether they are performed with sexual desire (شهوة). Without desire, the act does not indicate marital intimacy and is not a revocation.
The text cites examples: midwife, physician – touching without desire is permissible in non-marital contexts.
3
Apply the condition of specificity
For looking, it must be at the wife's private parts (فرج); looking at other parts (e.g., face) even with desire is not specific enough. For touching, it must be intimate (e.g., kissing).
Specificity ensures the act is unique to marriage.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that revocation is only valid by explicit speech, not by action. The thamarah al-khilaf: in the Hanafi school, intercourse during idda automatically revokes; in the Shafi'i school, it does not.
The Hanafi rationale: revocation is a continuation of ownership, not a new contract.

كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة
بنحو: راجعتك، وبوطئها، ومسها بشهوة، ونظره إلى فرجها بشهوة) : هذا عندنا، وأما عند الشافعي فلا تصح إلا بالقول.
[١]قوله : بنحو ؛ المراد به قول دال على الرد كراجعت، وراجعتك، ومسكتك، ورددتك، وأمسكتك وغير ذلك، وهذا كله لا يحتاج إلى نية ؛ لكونه صريحاً.
[٢]قوله : وبوطئها ؛ أي هذه صور الرجعة بالفعل، ولا تخلو عن كراهة فإن المستحب هو الرجعة بالقول، كما في «الفتح».
[٣]قوله : بشهوة ؛ إنّما اعتبر في المس قيد الشهوة، وكذا في النظر، وقيد النظر بكونه إلى فرجها، حتى لو مسها أو قبلها أو نظر إلى فرجها بلا شهوة، أو نظر إلى غير فرجها بشهوة لا يكون رجعة ; لأنّ كونَ الفعل رجعة إنّما هو لكونه دليلاً، والدليل إنما يكون ما يختص به، والنظر إلى الفرج بشهوة، والمس بشهوة والتقبيل بشهوة من الأفاعيل الخاصة بالنكاح، خصوصاً في حق الحرّة.
بخلاف المس والنظر إلى الفرج بغير شهوة ، فلأنه قد يحل بدون النكاح، كما في القابلة والطبيب والخاتنة والشاهد في الزنى، والنظر إلى غير الفرج قد يقع بين الساكنين في بيت. كذا في «الهداية» و«النهاية».
[٤] قوله : إلا بالقول ؛ أي إذا كان قادراً عليه، فإن كان أخرس أو معتقل اللسان تصح رجعته بالإشارة، ووجه ذلك : أنّ الرجعة بمنزلة ابتداء النكاح، فلا تصح بالفعل، ونحن نقول : إنّ حق الرجعة ثبت شرعاً نظراً للزوج ; ليمكنه التدارك عند الندم على المفارقة، وهذا يوجب استقلاله به ; ولذلك صحت الرجعة بلا رضاها اتفاقاً. وذلك يشعر بكون الرجعة استدامة للملك القائم لا إنشاء له من كل وجه،
ونُدِبَ إشهاده على الرّجعة وإعلامها بها ، وأن لا يدخل عليها حتى يستأذنها إن لم يقصد رجعتها.
ونُدِبَ إشهاده على الرّجعة وإعلامها بها) : أي إعلام الزوج إيَّاها بالرَّجعة، وأن لا يدخل عليها حتى يستأذنَها " إن لم يقصد رجعتها. والفعل وإن لم يكن كافياً في الإنشاء، فهو كافٍ في الاستدامة، ألا ترى أنه لو باع جارية على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام ثمّ وطئها، سقط الخيار كما يسقط بالقول. كذا في «العناية».
[ ۱ ] قوله : إشهاده ؛ بكسر الهمزة ; أي إقامة الشاهدين العدلين رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين، سواء كانت الرجعة قولاً أو فعلاً، احتراز عن التجاحد، وعن الوقوع في مواضع التهم ; لئلا يتهم بقعوده معها بعدما عرف أنه طلقها، والأمر في قوله : وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوَى عَدْلٍ مِنكُمْ ) للندب. كذا في «شرح الكنز» للزَّيْلَعِي.
[٢] قوله : وإعلامها ; أي إخبار الزوج معتد برجعته ; لئلا تنكح بعد العدة زوجاً غيره.
[٣] قوله : حتى يستأذنها ; ظاهره : أنه يكره له الدخول عليها في البيت إن لم تأذن له، وإنه لا يستحب الاستئذان إذا قصد الرجعة، وفي بعض النسخ حتى يؤذنها ; أي يعلمها بدخوله، بالتنحنح أو خفق نعليه أو النداء، وهو الموافق لما في «الهداية». والصحيح على ما في «البحر» وغيره : أنّ الاستئذان لا يستحب، وإنما يستحب الإيذان مطلقاً قصد الرجعة أو لم يقصد ; إذ على الأول لا يأمن من أن يرى الفرج بشهوة، فتكون رجعة بالفعل، وهو مكروه تنزيهاً مع عدم الإشهاد ، وهو أ أيضاً مكروه، وعلى الثاني ربّما يصير مراجعاً بالنظر من غير قصد، ثمّ يحتاج إلى تطليقها، فتطولُ العدة عليها.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation. [The husband revokes] by saying something like 'I revoke you,' and by having intercourse with her, and by touching her with desire, and by looking at her private parts with desire. This is our [Hanafi] position. As for al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except by speech. [1] His statement 'by something like' means a word indicating return, such as 'I return you,' 'I revoke you,' 'I hold you,' 'I take you back,' 'I keep you,' etc. All of this does not require intention because it is explicit. [2] His statement 'and by having intercourse with her' means these are the forms of revocation by action, and there is some dislike in it, for the recommended is revocation by speech, as in al-Fath. [3] His statement 'with desire' means that the condition of desire is considered for touch, similarly for looking, and the condition for looking is that it is at her private parts. So if he touches her or kisses her or looks at her private parts without desire, or looks at other than her private parts with desire, it is not a revocation. Because an act being a revocation is due to it being an indication, and an indication is only what is specific [to marriage]. Looking at the private parts with desire, touching with desire, and kissing with desire are acts specific to marriage, especially concerning a free woman. Unlike touching and looking at private parts without desire, because that may be permitted without marriage, as with a midwife, physician, circumciser, and witness to adultery. Looking at other than private parts may occur between cohabitants in a house. Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah. [4] His statement 'except by speech' means if he is capable of it. If he is mute or has a tied tongue, his revocation is valid by gesture. The reasoning behind this: revocation is like initiating marriage, so it is not valid by action. But we say: the right of revocation is established by law out of consideration for the husband, so that he can correct himself when regretting separation, and this necessitates his independence in it; therefore revocation is valid without her consent by agreement. That indicates that revocation is a continuation of existing ownership, not a complete new initiation. And it is recommended that he have witnesses to the revocation and inform her of it, and that he not enter upon her until he seeks her permission if he did not intend revocation. [1] His statement 'having witnesses' (with kasra on the alif) means producing two just witnesses – two men or a man and two women – whether the revocation is by speech or action, in order to avoid denial and falling into suspicion, lest he be accused of staying with her after he is known to have divorced her. The command in His statement 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' is recommended. Thus in Sharh al-Kanz by al-Zayla'i. [2] His statement 'and informing her' means the husband tells her that he is considered to have revoked her, so that she does not marry another husband after the waiting period. [3] His statement 'until he seeks her permission' its apparent meaning is that it is disliked for him to enter upon her in the house if she does not permit him, and it is not recommended to seek permission when he intends revocation. In some copies it is 'until he informs her,' i.e., he makes his entry known to her by clearing his throat, shuffling his sandals, or calling, and this is in agreement with what is in al-Hidayah. The correct view according to al-Bahr and others is that seeking permission is not recommended; only informing is recommended in all cases, whether he intends revocation or not. For in the first case, he is not safe from seeing the private parts with desire, which would constitute a revocation by action, which is disliked with the lack of witnesses, and that is also disliked. In the second case, he might become a revoker by looking unintentionally, then he would need to divorce her, and her waiting period would be prolonged.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the means by which a husband can revoke his divorce (رجعة) according to the Hanafi school. The author opens by stating the Hanafi position: revocation is valid either by explicit verbal statements (e.g., 'I revoke you') or by specific physical acts done with desire, such as intercourse, touching, kissing, or looking at the wife's private parts. These acts serve as indications of the desire to resume the marriage. The Shafi'i position is noted as requiring speech only. The commentary then breaks down each element of the matn. Words of revocation are explicit and do not require intention. Acts of revocation are allowed but disliked; the recommended method is speech. The condition of desire is crucial: without desire, an act does not indicate marital intimacy and therefore does not constitute revocation. Similarly, looking at non-private parts even with desire is not specific enough, as it can occur in non-marital contexts (e.g., between housemates). An exception is made for a mute husband, who may revoke by gesture. The underlying rationale (talil) is that revocation is a continuation of existing marriage ownership, not a new contract, and it is established for the husband's benefit to allow reconciliation without the wife's consent. The passage then discusses recommended practices: having two witnesses and informing the wife. Witnessing is recommended to avoid denial and accusations. Informing the wife is recommended to prevent her from marrying another after the waiting period. Regarding entering the house, the correct view is that the husband should simply announce his entry (by clearing throat etc.) rather than seeking permission, especially if he does not intend revocation, to avoid unintentional revocation by looking with desire. This careful reasoning shows the Hanafi concern for practical consequences and the integrity of marital bonds.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-rajʿah): The husband's right to take back his wife during her waiting period after a revocable divorce, without a new marriage contract.
وطئها (waṭʾu-hā): Sexual intercourse with the wife; one of the physical acts that constitute revocation if done with desire.
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; a necessary condition for physical acts (touching, looking) to count as revocation.
إشهاد (ishhād): Having two just witnesses (two men or a man and two women) to the revocation, whether by speech or action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following conditions is necessary for a physical act (e.g., touching or looking) to count as a valid revocation (رجعة)?
A) The wife's consent
B) The presence of two witnesses
C) The act being performed with sexual desire (شهوة)
D) The act being witnessed by a judge

Key Takeaway
Revocation by action is valid in the Hanafi school if the act is done with desire and is specific to marriage.

Recommendation of Witnesses and Informing in Revocation
Statement of recommended acts in revocation with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses and informing in revocation, identify the Quranic dalil, and explain why the imperative is interpreted as recommendation (nadb) rather than obligation.

Core Idea
Witnesses and informing in revocation are recommended (mandub), not obligatory, because the Quranic command is interpreted as nadb.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the recommended acts when a husband revokes his divorce. Understanding the distinction between obligation and recommendation is crucial for correct practice. We will examine the evidence from the Quran and the Hanafi reasoning that makes these acts mandub rather than wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended acts
The passage states that having two just witnesses (ishhad) and informing the wife (i'lam) are recommended. Also, entering without permission is not recommended unless he intends revocation, in which case mere notification is recommended.
These are sunan (recommended), not arkan or shurut.
2
Locate the Quranic evidence
The author cites verse 65:2: 'وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوَى عَدْلٍ مِنكُمْ' (And bring to witness two just men from among you). This is the primary dalil.
The verse uses the imperative form, which in Hanafi usul can indicate obligation or recommendation depending on context.
3
Apply Hanafi usul: imperative as nadb
The Hanafi school interprets this command as recommendation (nadb), not obligation, due to other evidence (e.g., the validity of revocation without witnesses and the principle that commands after prohibitions indicate permissibility). Thus, omitting witnesses does not invalidate the revocation.
This is a key difference from some other schools that consider witnessing obligatory.
4
Understand the practical implications
Revocation is valid without witnesses or informing, but it is recommended to have witnesses to avoid denial or accusations, and to inform the wife so she does not remarry after the waiting period.
The recommendation serves social and legal caution.
5
Note the intra-madhhab nuance on seeking permission
The correct view is that seeking permission to enter is not recommended; rather, mere notification (e.g., clearing throat) is recommended in all cases to prevent accidental revocation through seeing the wife with desire.
This avoids prolonging the waiting period unwittingly.

And it is recommended to have witnesses for the revocation and to inform her of it, and that he should not enter upon her until he seeks her permission if he did not intend her revocation. And it is recommended to have witnesses for the revocation and to inform her of it: i.e., informing the wife of the revocation, and that he should not enter upon her until he seeks her permission if he did not intend her revocation. An action, even if it is not sufficient for initiation, is sufficient for continuance; do you not see that if he sold a slave-girl on the condition that he has the option for three days, then he had intercourse with her, the option is nullified just as it is nullified by speech. Thus in al-'Inayah. [1] His saying: 'ishhaduhu (with kasra on the hamza): i.e., establishing two just witnesses, two men or one man and two women, whether the revocation is by speech or action, as a precaution against denial and against falling into places of suspicion; lest he be accused of staying with her after it is known that he divorced her. And the command in His saying: 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' is for recommendation. Thus in Sharh al-Kanz by al-Zayla'i. [2] His saying: 'and informing her': i.e., informing the wife that he is a person whose revocation counts; lest she marry another husband after the waiting period. [3] His saying: 'until he seeks her permission': its apparent meaning is that it is disliked for him to enter upon her in the house if she does not permit him, and that seeking permission is not recommended if he intends revocation. And in some copies: 'until he informs her'; i.e., he notifies her of his entry, by clearing his throat, or shuffling his sandals, or calling, and this is consistent with what is in al-Hidayah. And the correct opinion according to what is in al-Bahr and others is that seeking permission is not recommended; rather, notification is recommended absolutely, whether he intends revocation or not; because in the first case, he is not safe from seeing the private part with desire, resulting in an actual revocation, and it is slightly disliked without witnesses, and that is also disliked; and in the second case, perhaps he would become a revocator by looking without intention, then need to divorce her, so the waiting period is prolonged for her.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the Quran. The author states that it is recommended (mandub) to have two just witnesses for the revocation, to inform the wife of the revocation, and that the husband should not enter upon her without her permission if he does not intend revocation. The commentary explains that 'ishhad' means establishing witnesses, whether the revocation is by speech or action, to avoid denial and suspicion. The Quranic verse (At-Talaq 65:2) 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' is cited, and the command is interpreted as recommendation (nadb), not obligation, according to the Hanafi school. Informing the wife prevents her from marrying another after the waiting period. Regarding seeking permission, the correct view is that it is not recommended; rather, mere notification (i.e., announcing one's entry) is recommended in all cases, because if he intends revocation, entering without permission might lead to seeing private parts with desire, causing an actual revocation (which is disliked without witnesses). If he does not intend revocation, looking could accidentally cause revocation, prolonging her waiting period.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Establishing witnesses (two men or one man and two women) for the act of revocation.
رجعة (ruj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the waiting period, whether by speech or action.
إعلام (i'lam): Informing the wife that she has been revoked, to prevent her from marrying another after the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Disliked (makruh)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, having witnesses and informing the wife in revocation are recommended, not obligatory; the revocation remains valid without them.

Claim of Revocation After Waiting Period: Ruling and Oath Requirement
Ruling on claim of revocation after waiting period depending on wife's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's claim of revocation after the waiting period and explain why no oath is required from the wife when she denies.

Core Idea
If wife confirms, revocation stands; if she denies, revocation fails without an oath from her.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical procedural rule in family law: the claim of revocation (رجعة) after the waiting period. The ruling hinges on the wife's response and, importantly, the absence of an oath requirement for her according to Abu Hanifa. Let's break down the two scenarios and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The husband claims he revoked during the iddah after it has ended. The wife either confirms or denies. Note also a variation: he says 'I have revoked you' now, and she says 'my iddah has passed'.
2
State the ruling upon confirmation
If the wife confirms the claim, the revocation is valid – it is treated as a proper ruj'ah.
This is by mutual agreement.
3
State the ruling upon denial
If the wife denies, the revocation is not established. Moreover, she does not have to take an oath.
This is the Hanafi position: لا يمين عليها عند أبي حنيفة.
4
Explain the reason for no oath
Revocation is among the seven matters where oaths are not administered according to Abu Hanifa.
These matters include nikah, ruj'ah, fay', ila', istilad, waqf, nasab, wala'.
5
Handle the 'my iddah has passed' scenario
If the husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife says 'my iddah has passed', and the timeframe allows for iddah completion, she is believed and revocation fails. If timeframe is too short, she is not believed.
This is from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ادعى بعد العدة الرّجعة فيها ،وصدقته فهو رجعة ، وإن كذبته فلا، ولا يمين عليها عند أبي حنيفة له ، وإن قال : راجعتك ، فقالت : مضت عدتي فلا رجعة ولو ادعى بعد العدة الرّجعة فيها وصدقته ، فهو رجعة ، وإن كذبته فلا (1) ولا يمين عليها عند أبي حنيفة ) ؛ فإن الرَّجعة من الأشياء التي لا يمين فيها عند أبي حنيفة .
[0] ( وإن قال : راجعتك ، فقالت : مضت عدتي فلا رجعة) : أي إن كانت (10) المدة مدة تحتمل انقضاء العدَّة ، فالمرأةُ تصدَّق في إخبارها بانقضاء العدة
And if he claims after the waiting period the revocation during it, and she confirms him, then it is a revocation; but if she denies him, then no, and there is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa. And if he says: 'I have revoked you,' and she says: 'My waiting period has passed,' then no revocation. And if he claims after the waiting period the revocation during it and she confirms him, then it is a revocation; but if she denies him, then no, and there is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa; for revocation is among the matters in which there is no oath according to Abu Hanifa. [0] And if he says: 'I have revoked you,' and she says: 'My waiting period has passed,' then no revocation: i.e., if the period is one that can accommodate the expiration of the waiting period, then the woman is believed in her report of the expiration of the waiting period.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the main text with commentary. It addresses two scenarios. First: If the husband claims after the waiting period that he had revoked during it, and the wife confirms, then the revocation is established by their mutual agreement. If she denies, then no revocation is established, and according to Abu Hanifa, the wife does not take an oath (i.e., she is not required to swear that the revocation did not occur) because revocation is among the matters where oaths are not administered. The commentary explains that revocation is one of seven matters (nikah, ruj'ah, fay', ila', istilad, waqf, nasab, wala') where no oath is required. Second: If the husband says 'I have revoked you' (intending initiation) and the wife says 'my waiting period has passed,' then no revocation is established, provided that the time frame could actually allow the waiting period to end (e.g., three menstrual cycles). If the time frame is too short, the wife is not believed.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): Oath required from a party in a dispute; here, no oath is required from the wife in revocation cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband claims revocation after the waiting period and the wife denies, what happens?
A) The revocation is established and the wife must swear
B) The revocation is not established and the wife takes no oath
C) The husband is believed and the wife must swear
D) The wife is believed and the husband must swear

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife's denial of a husband's claim of revocation after the waiting period is accepted without an oath, because revocation belongs to the category of matters where oaths are not administered.

Husband Claims Revocation, Wife Claims Waiting Period Ended
Ruling on revocation dispute with time and oath conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband claims revocation but the wife claims the waiting period has ended, and apply the condition of time sufficiency and the rule on oaths.

Core Idea
The wife is believed about her 'iddah only if sufficient time has elapsed for three menstrual cycles.

In revocable divorce, timing is critical. If the husband claims he revoked during the 'iddah but the wife asserts the 'iddah has already expired, the outcome hinges on whether enough time has passed for three cycles. The Hanafi school balances evidentiary presumptions with the wife's personal knowledge of her cycles. This lesson unpacks that legal calculus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting claims
The husband says 'I have revoked you' (intending initiation). The wife responds 'My waiting period has passed.' Their statements are contradictory regarding the existence of a valid revocation.
The husband's intention matters: if he meant to inform of a past revocation, the earlier rule (confirmation by wife) applies.
2
Check the time frame
Determine whether the time elapsed since the divorce could accommodate three menstrual cycles. If yes, the wife's claim is plausible; if not, it is implausible.
Default 'iddah for a free woman is three cycles; for a pregnant woman, it ends at delivery.
3
Apply the rule on belief
If time is sufficient, the wife is believed with her oath – no revocation. If time is insufficient, the wife is not believed and revocation stands (unless she brings evidence).
If the wife refuses to take an oath, revocation is established by default.
4
Handle the mutual confirmation exception
If the wife confirms the husband's claim (even after time), revocation is established by their mutual confirmation, regardless of actual truth (diyanatan vs. qadaan).
This mirrors the rule for marriage – mutual acknowledgment creates legal effect.

And if he says: 'I have revoked you,' and she says: 'My waiting period has passed,' then no revocation. And if he claims after the waiting period the revocation during it and she confirms him, then it is a revocation; but if she denies him, then no, and there is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa; for revocation is among the matters in which there is no oath according to Abu Hanifa. [0] (And if he says: 'I have revoked you,' and she says: 'My waiting period has passed,' then no revocation): i.e., if the period is one that can accommodate the expiration of the waiting period, then the woman is believed in her report of the expiration of the waiting period. [1] His saying: 'no': meaning if the husband says after the waiting period: 'I had revoked you by action or speech during the waiting period' and the wife confirms him, the revocation is established by their mutual agreement, just as marriage is established by their mutual agreement. Yes, if they are both lying in reality, it is not established religiously (diyanatan). And if she denies him, the revocation is not established unless the husband brings evidence and she swears before them (the judges). If he does not bring evidence, she does not swear. [2] His saying: 'from among the matters': they are seven: marriage, revocation, fay' (return to wife during ila'), ila', istilad (acknowledging paternity of a slave-concubine), waqf, lineage, and patronage; its detail is sought from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) of this book. [3] His saying: 'I have revoked you': intending initiation; for if he intended information, it is the previous issue, which refers to her confirmation. [4] His saying: 'and she said': he indicated with the particle 'fa' that her response occurred connectedly; so if she remained silent then said: 'My waiting period expired,' the revocation is valid by agreement, because she is suspected due to her silence and lack of immediate response. And if she began by saying: 'My waiting period expired,' then the husband said: 'I have revoked you,' the saying is hers by agreement. And if both statements occurred together, the revocation is not established either. Thus in al-Fath and al-Nahr. [0] His saying: 'i.e., if it was... etc.': if the period cannot accommodate the expiration of the waiting period, which is three menstrual cycles, for example if their speech occurs before the passage of one month or half of it from the time of divorce, she is not believed in her statement, because the apparent reality belies her. [6] His saying: 'she is believed': i.e., with her oath; so if she refuses to swear, the revocation is established based on the continuation of the waiting period.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the scenario where the husband says 'I have revoked you' (intending initiation) and the wife responds 'My waiting period has passed.' The ruling depends on the time frame: if sufficient time has elapsed for the waiting period to end (three menstrual cycles), the woman is believed in her statement that her 'iddah has expired, and no revocation occurs. If insufficient time has passed, she is not believed because the apparent reality contradicts her. The commentary further explains that if she denies the revocation, the husband must bring evidence and she takes an oath; if she refuses the oath, revocation is established. Also, the response must be immediate; if she remains silent then speaks, the revocation is valid by agreement. The list of seven matters where no oath is required is reiterated: nikah, ruj'ah, fay', ila', istilad, waqf, and nasab/wala'.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce; for a woman divorced by revocable divorce, it is three menstrual cycles.
تصديق (tasdiq): Confirmation; here, if the wife confirms the husband's claim of revocation, it is established.

Post-Iddah Revocation Claim: Wife's Confirmation and Denial
Ruling on post-iddah revocation claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband claims revocation after iddah and the wife confirms or denies, and apply the Hanafi position on oaths in this matter.

Core Idea
A post-iddah revocation claim is valid only if the wife confirms; if she denies, the husband must prove it with evidence, and no oath is required from her.

This passage presents a nuanced ruling on post-iddah revocation claims, balancing the legal effect of the wife's confirmation or denial. It also addresses the timing of statements and introduces a key Hanafi distinction: revocation is among matters where no oath is administered. This lesson unpacks the conditions for validity and the role of the wife's testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The husband claims after the iddah has ended that he had revoked the divorce during the iddah period.
This is a retrospective claim; the revocation is not happening now but is alleged to have occurred earlier.
2
Determine the effect of wife's response
If the wife confirms the claim, the revocation is valid (it is a raj'ah). If she denies, the revocation is not established unless the husband brings proof (witnesses).
This parallels the rule for marriage establishment by mutual confirmation.
3
Note the oath exception
According to Abu Hanifa, no oath is taken from the wife in revocation matters, regardless of her denial. This is because revocation is categorized among matters where oaths are not administered.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ and require an oath, but the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Analyze the timing nuance
If the wife claims that her iddah has already passed at the time of the husband's statement (e.g., he says 'I revoked you' and she immediately says 'My iddah has passed'), her word is believed only if the elapsed time could realistically accommodate three full menstrual cycles.
If only a month has passed, her claim is rejected because it contradicts apparent reality.
5
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The commentary also discusses variations: if she remains silent before answering, her later claim is accepted; if she speaks first, her word is taken; if both speak simultaneously, no revocation is established.
These details highlight the importance of immediacy and consistency in legal statements.

Indeed, if they were lying in reality, it is not established religiously. Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a husband claiming to have revoked the divorce after the iddah period has ended. The ruling is straightforward: if the wife confirms the husband's claim, the revocation is valid, just as marriage is confirmed by mutual confirmation. If she denies, there is no revocation unless the husband brings evidence. Notably, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the wife is not required to take an oath in this matter because revocation is categorized among matters where oaths are not administered. The commentary then elaborates on a specific scenario: if the husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife immediately responds that her iddah has passed, the revocation is not valid. This is provided that the time elapsed could realistically accommodate the completion of the iddah (three menstrual cycles). The wife's statement is believed because she is the one who knows her own iddah. However, if the iddah could not have ended (e.g., only a month has passed since divorce), her claim is rejected because the apparent reality contradicts her. The commentary also covers variations in the timing of statements: if she remains silent before answering, her later claim is accepted due to suspicion; if she speaks first, her word is accepted; if both speak simultaneously, no revocation is established. Finally, it notes that if the wife takes an oath and refuses it, the revocation is established on the basis that the iddah likely continues.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce during the waiting period (iddah).
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The revocation is valid if the husband takes an oath.
B) The revocation is invalid unless the husband brings proof, and the wife does not take an oath.
C) The revocation is valid only if the wife takes an oath.
D) The revocation is invalid regardless.

Key Takeaway
A post-iddah revocation claim hinges on the wife's response: confirmation validates it, denial invalidates it without proof, and Abu Hanifa exempts the wife from oath in this matter.

Slave Girl's Revocation: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence: slave-wife vs master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the slave-girl's revocation dispute and explain why Abu Hanifa prioritizes the slave-girl's word over the master's.

Core Idea
The slave-girl's word on her iddah overrides the master's claim of revocation.

This passage addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a divorced slave-girl's husband claims he revoked the divorce during her iddah, her master confirms, but she denies. Whose word is accepted? The answer hinges on who holds exclusive knowledge of the iddah. Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) diverge, offering a rich study in how evidence and trustworthiness interact in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
Recognize the four actors: the husband (claims revocation), the slave-wife (denies), the master (confirms), and the context of iddah. Note that the husband's claim of revocation is made after the iddah period has supposedly ended.
The husband informs the master, not the wife directly.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that the master's confession (iẓhār) of a right that is purely his (i.e., the marital contract and its consequences) is accepted. They analogize to the master's acceptance of marriage acknowledgment.
They focus on the master's ownership right.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that revocation is tied to the existence of iddah, and knowledge of iddah belongs exclusively to the woman. She is 'trusted' (amīnah) regarding her iddah, as per Quran 2:228. Thus, her denial of revocation is taken, making the revocation invalid.
The Quranic verse prohibits women from concealing what is in their wombs.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the revocation is not valid, so the divorce stands and the woman is free to remarry after iddah. Under the Sahibayn, the revocation is valid, and the marriage resumes.
The difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is the continuation or dissolution of the marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما في زوج أمةٍ أَخْبَرَ بعد العدة بالرجعة فيها لسيدها وهذا عند أبي حنيفة [رضي الله عنه؟]، وأما عندهما فتصح الرجعة ؛ لأنها لم تخبر قبل الرجعة بانقضاء العدة، فالظاهر بقاؤها.
(كما في زوج أمةٍ أَخْبَرَ بعد العدة بالرجعة فيها لسيدها)
والفرق لأبي حنيفة [رضي الله عنه؟] بين ما هاهنا وبين ما مرّ حيث لا تحلف عنده أن إلزام اليمين لفائدة النكول، وهو بذل عنده، والرجعة ونحوها من الأشياء التي لا تحليف فيها، لا يجري فيها البذل، وأما العدة فهي امتناع عن التزوّج، والاحتباس في منزل الزوج، وهو يمكن جريان البذل فيه، فلذلك يجب عليه اليمين هاهنا. كذا حققه الزيلعي وابن الهمام ).
[1] قوله : فالظاهر بقاؤها ؛ لأنّ الظاهر بقاء ما كان على ما كان، ولو كانت انقضت قبله لأخبرته، وفي الإخبار عنه حين قوله : راجعتك تتهم. والجواب عنه من قبله : إنّ التهمة هاهنا لا تعتبر فإنها أمينة في حال نفسها، وتقبلُ قولها فيه يدل عليه قوله : وَلا يَحِلُّ لَهُنَّ أَن يَكْتُمْنَ مَا خَلَقَ اللَّهُ فِي أَرْحَامِهِنَّ فلما أخبرت بانقضاء العدّة والإخبارُ لا بد له من سابقية زمان المخبر عنه، وأقرب أحواله حال قول الزوج ؛ لعدم الدليل على مقدار معين، فيجعل كأن العدة انقضت مقارناً لقوله : راجعتك، فلا تصح الرجعة.
[٢]قوله : كما في زوج ... الخ؛ صورته : إن أمةً لرجل زوجها من رجل فطلقها الزوج وأخبر سيد زوجته بعد انقضاء العدّة أنّه راجعها قبل انقضائها، فصدقه السيد وكذبته الأمة المعتدة، فالقول قوله عندهما، وقولها عنده.
لهما : إنّه أقرّ بما هو خالص حقه فيقبل، كما لو أقرّ عليها بالنكاح.
وله : أن صحة الرجعة وعدمها مبني على قيام العدة وانقضائها، وهي أمينة مصدقة بالأخبار بالانقضاء، والبقاء لا قول للمولى فيها أصلاً، فكذا فيما يبتني عليها، وإنما يقبل قوله في النكاح لاستبداده به بخلاف الرجعة، كذا في «النهر».
As in the case of the husband of a slave-girl: he informed after the iddah of the revocation therein to her master, and this according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him), but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the revocation is valid because she did not inform before the revocation that the iddah had ended, so the apparent is its continuation. (As in the case of the husband of a slave-girl: he informed after the iddah of the revocation therein to her master). And the distinction according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) between what is here and what preceded, where she does not take an oath according to him, is that imposing an oath is for the benefit of refusal, which is financial payment according to him, and revocation and similar matters where no oath is administered do not involve financial payment. As for the iddah, it is abstaining from marriage and confinement in the husband's house, and it is possible for financial payment to occur therein, so he must take an oath here. Thus verified by al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam. [1] His statement: 'so the apparent is its continuation' because the apparent is that what was remains as it was, and if it had ended before, she would have informed him, and in informing about it at the time of his saying 'I have revoked you' she is accused. The answer from him is that the accusation here is not considered because she is entrusted in her own case, and her statement is accepted in it, as indicated by His statement: 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs.' So when she informed of the completion of the iddah, and the report must have a prior time for the reported thing, and the nearest condition is the time of the husband's statement, because there is no evidence for a specific amount, it is made as if the iddah ended concurrently with his saying 'I have revoked you', so the revocation is not valid. [2] His statement: 'as in the husband...' etc.; its form: There is a slave-girl belonging to a man who married her to a man, then the husband divorced her and informed the master of his wife after the completion of the iddah that he had revoked her before its completion. The master believed him but the slave-girl (the wife in iddah) denied him. So the word is his (the master's) according to them (Sahibayn), and her word according to him (Abu Hanifa). For them: He (the master) has confessed what is purely his right, so it is accepted, just as if he confessed marriage over her. For him: The validity or invalidity of the revocation is based on the existence or completion of the iddah, and she is entrusted and believed in reporting the completion, and the master has no say in it at all, so similarly in what is based upon it. And his statement is accepted in marriage because he is independent in it, unlike revocation. So it is in al-Nahr.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute involving a scenario where a slave-girl is married, then divorced, and her husband claims after the iddah that he had revoked the divorce during the iddah. He informs the master (owner) of the slave-girl, who confirms the claim, but the slave-girl herself denies it. According to Abu Hanifa, the word of the slave-girl is taken, meaning the revocation is not established. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the word of the master is taken, and the revocation is valid. The reasoning: The Sahibayn argue that the master is confessing to a right that is purely his (since he owns the slave-girl), similar to him acknowledging a marriage contract over her. Abu Hanifa counters that the validity of revocation depends on the existence of the iddah, and the slave-girl is the one entrusted to know about her own iddah (as per Quran 2:228). The master has no say in whether the iddah has ended or not. Therefore, the slave-girl's denial that the revocation occurred is effective. The passage also discusses the distinction between this case and the earlier one concerning a free woman: in this case, the slave-girl is not required to take an oath because revocation is among matters where oaths are not administered, but in the earlier iddah case, oath is relevant because it involves financial implications (like maintenance during iddah).

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave who is married.
مولى (mawla): Master or owner of a slave, here the owner of the slave-girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave-girl whose husband claims revocation after iddah, and her master confirms but the slave-girl denies, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The revocation is valid because the master confirmed.
B) The revocation is not valid because the slave-girl denied.
C) The revocation is valid only if the master and husband take an oath.
D) The revocation is not valid unless the slave-girl takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position that the slave-girl's word prevails over the master's in revocation cases rests on the Quranic principle that women are trustworthy regarding their own iddah.
