Raj'a of a Slave Woman: Whose Word is Accepted?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance of word in raj'a for a slave woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on acceptance of word in raj'a of a slave woman and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The slave woman's word about her own idda is accepted over the master's claim of raj'a — she is 'amina' in her own condition.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave woman's idda and the validity of a husband's revocation (raj'a). The central question is: when the husband claims to have revoked the divorce after the idda, and the slave woman claims the idda has ended, whose word is accepted? The Hanafi school splits between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, with important practical consequences for the woman's marital status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whose word is accepted in this scenario.
Abu Hanifa says word of the slave woman; Sahibayn say word of the master.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's proof
Follow the reasoning of the Sahibayn: the master has exclusive authority over the marriage of his slave girl, so his admission of raj'a is accepted, like his admission to marriage.
Analogy to master's admission of marriage.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the idda is solely the woman's concern—she is trustworthy (amina) based on Quran 2:228—so her word takes precedence over the master's.
The master has no say in the idda itself.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: if the woman's word is accepted, she can remarry immediately; if the master's word is accepted, the raj'a is valid and she remains married.
This directly affects her marital status and rights.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Note that the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as upheld in major works like al-Hidayah.
Why this view is given precedence: textual evidence (Quran) and the nature of idda as a personal condition.

[٢]قوله : كما في زوج ... الخ ؛ صورته : إن أمةً لرجل زوجها من رجل فطلقها الزوج وأخبر سيد زوجته بعد انقضاء العدّة أنّه راجعها قبل انقضائها، فصدقه السيد وكذبته الأمة المعتدة، فالقول قوله عندهما، وقولها عنده. لهما : إنّه أقرّ بما هو خالص حقه فيقبل، كما لو أقرّ عليها بالنكاح. وله : أن صحة الرجعة وعدمها مبني على قيام العدة وانقضائها، وهي أمينة مصدقة بالأخبار بالانقضاء، والبقاء لا قول للمولى فيها أصلاً، فكذا فيما يبتني عليها، وإنما يقبل قوله في النكاح لاستبداده به بخلاف الرجعة، كذا في «النهر». فصدقه وكذبته)؛ فإنَّ القول قولها عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما فالقولُ قول المولى، أو قال : راجعتك فقالت : مضت عدتي وأنكرا. [١] قوله : فصدقه ؛ ولو كذبه المولى وصدقته الأمة فالقول للمولى اتفاقاً، وقيل: هو على الخلاف أيضاً، والصحيح أنه اتفاقي، والفرقُ لأبي حنيفة له أنّ الأمة منقضية العدّة في الحال ؛ لأنّ الفرض أنّ الزوج أخبره بعدها ، فثبت حق المولى في ملك المتعة ، فلا يقبل قولها في إبطاله، بخلاف الوجه الأوّل، فإنّ المولى بالتصديق في الرجعة مقرّ بقيام العدة عند الرجعة، ولا يظهر ملك المولى مع العدة. كذا في «الهداية». [٢]قوله : فقالت : يعني قال زوج الأمة : راجعتك مريداً به إنشاء الرجعة، فقالت فوراً : مضت عدتي، وأنكر الزوج وسيّد الأمة مضي العدة، فالقول قولها عنده لما مرّ. كذا في «الهداية».
As in the case of a husband of a slave woman who informed her master after the idda that he had revoked (raj'a) her – this is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the raj'a is valid, because she did not inform before the raj'a of the expiration of the idda, so the presumption is its continuance. (As in the case of a husband of a slave woman who informed the master after the idda of the raj'a – and the distinction for Abu Hanifa between this and what passed is that he does not make her swear an oath there, because imposing an oath is for the benefit of nukul, which is a payment according to him, and raj'a and similar matters in which there is no swearing, payment does not apply. As for the idda, it is abstention from marriage and confinement in the husband's home, and it is possible for payment to apply, so the oath is obligatory here. Thus verified by al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam.) [1] His saying: 'So the presumption is its continuance' – because the presumption is that what was continues as it was. If it had expired before, she would have informed him; and in informing about it at the time of his saying 'I have revoked you', she is accused. The response from him is that accusation here is not considered, for she is trustworthy regarding her own condition, and her word is accepted therein, as indicated by His saying: 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs' (2:228). So when she informs of the expiration of the idda, and informing necessarily requires a prior time of the reported matter, the closest state is the time of the husband's statement, due to lack of evidence for a specific duration. So it is as if the idda expired concurrent with his saying 'I have revoked you', so the raj'a is not valid. [2] His saying: 'As in the case of a husband...' etc. Its form: A slave woman belonging to a man was married to another man; the husband divorced her, and after the expiration of the idda he informed her master that he had revoked her before its expiration. The master believed him, but the slave woman in idda denied it. Then the word is according to the master according to the two, and according to her according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to them: He admitted to what is his exclusive right, so it is accepted, just as if he admitted to marriage with her. And according to him: The validity or invalidity of raj'a is based on the continuance or expiration of the idda, and she is trustworthy, believed in informing of expiration, and the master has no say in the idda at all, so likewise in what is based on it. He (the master) is only accepted in marriage because he has sole authority over it, unlike raj'a. Thus in 'al-Nahr'. (So if he believes him and she denies him) – then the word is hers according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two, the word is the master's. Or he said: 'I have revoked you', and she said: 'My idda has ended', and they both deny. [1] His saying: 'So if he believes him' – and if the master denies him and the slave woman believes him, the word is the master's unanimously; and it is said it is still based on the disagreement, but the correct view is that it is unanimous. The distinction for Abu Hanifa is that the slave woman's idda has ended at present, because it is assumed that the husband informed him after it, so the master's right to ownership of enjoyment is established, so her word cannot be accepted to nullify it, unlike the first case, for the master, by believing in the raj'a, is affirming the continuance of the idda at the time of raj'a, and the master's ownership does not appear with the idda. Thus in 'al-Hidaya'. [2] His saying: 'So she said' – meaning the husband of the slave woman said: 'I have revoked you', intending thereby the initiation of raj'a, and she immediately said: 'My idda has ended', and the husband and the slave woman's master denied the expiration of the idda, then the word is hers according to him for the reason mentioned. Thus in 'al-Hidaya'.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whose word is accepted in matters of raj'a (revocation of divorce) when the wife is a slave woman and the idda period may have expired. The text presents several scenarios:

First scenario (from the beginning): The husband of a slave woman informs her master after the idda that he has revoked the divorce within the idda. According to Abu Hanifa, the word of the slave woman (that the idda has ended) is accepted, and thus the raj'a is invalid. According to Sahibayn, the master's word is accepted because the wife did not inform before the raj'a that the idda had ended, so the presumption is that the idda continues, and thus the raj'a is valid.

The commentary explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa relies on the Quranic verse (2:228) which establishes that women are trustworthy regarding their idda and what is in their wombs. The woman is 'amina' (trustworthy) in her own condition, so her declaration that the idda has ended is accepted. The presumption of continuity of idda is overridden by her statement. The Sahibayn, on the other hand, argue that the master has exclusive right over the marriage of his slave girl, so his acknowledgement of raj'a is accepted as an admission of his right.

The commentary also distinguishes this case from a previous case involving an oath (yameen). In the previous case, an oath was required because it involved a financial matter (bazl), whereas raj'a is not subject to oath because it does not involve payment. However, in the present case, because the idda involves confinement and prevention of marriage, it can involve payment, so an oath is imposed.

Further, the text discusses a scenario where the husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife immediately responds that her idda has ended. The word is given to the wife according to Abu Hanifa. But if the master believes the husband and the wife denies, the ruling is according to the earlier difference: master's word accepted by Sahibayn, wife's word by Abu Hanifa.

The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the detailed defense and the consistent preference in the Hanafi school for the woman's word in matters of idda.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'a): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the idda period, which restores the marriage without a new contract.
عدة (idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
أمة (ama): A female slave. In this context, she is married and then divorced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The husband's word is accepted.
B) The master's word is accepted.
C) The slave woman's word is accepted.
D) Both the husband's and master's words are accepted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the slave woman's word about her own idda is accepted over the master's claim of raj'a, as she is considered trustworthy.

When Does the Idda End: Cessation of Last Menstrual Blood
Ruling on completion of idda when the last menstrual blood stops

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when the idda is complete based on the last menstrual blood, and distinguish between cessation at ten days and cessation before ten days.

Core Idea
Idda ends immediately when last menstrual blood stops at ten days; if less, it requires ghusl or passing of a prayer time.

This lesson addresses a critical question in family law: exactly when does the waiting period (idda) after a revocable divorce come to an end? The ruling hinges on the maximum duration of menstruation (hayd) in the Hanafi school—ten days. Understanding this distinction protects both spouses' rights and eliminates uncertainty about marital status. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the precise moment the idda is complete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the blood in question
The 'last idda blood' is the final menstruation in the sequence of quru': the third for a free woman, the second for a slave woman. This blood determines when the idda ends.
Ensure you know the number of quru' required for each type of woman.
2
Determine the duration of bleeding
Check if the blood stopped at exactly ten days or before ten days. If it stopped at ten days, the maximum hayd duration in the Hanafi school is reached, meaning the blood is definitely hayd and has ended.
If bleeding exceeds ten days, it becomes istihada, but that is a separate case.
3
Apply the rule for cessation at ten days
If the blood stops after ten days (i.e., at the completion of the maximum hayd), the idda ends immediately upon cessation. No further confirmation is needed because the woman is certain to have left hayd.
Ten days is the absolute limit for menstrual blood in the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the rule for cessation before ten days
If the blood stops before ten days, the idda does not end immediately. Since blood could return within the ten-day hayd window, the cessation must be confirmed by either performing ghusl (ritual bath) after the blood stops, or by the passing of a fard prayer time without having performed ghusl.
The passing of a prayer time (e.g., Zuhr, Asr) serves as a legal indication of purity through the lapse of time.
5
Conclude the idda's end and effect on raj'a
Once the idda is complete, the husband's right of raj'a (revocation of divorce) is cut off. The woman becomes free to remarry.
This ruling applies to revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). For irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in), different rules apply but are not discussed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن انقطع دمُ آخرِ العدةِ لعشرة أيام تمت ولأقل منها لا ، حتى تغتسل ، أو يمضي عليها وقتُ فرض. قوله : دم آخر العدة ؛ وهو الحيضُ الثاني في الأمة، والثالث في الحرة، فإنّ عدة الحرة ثلاثة قروء، وعدّة الأمة حيضتان، والحاصل أن الرجعة تنقطع بتمام العدة، وهو يكون بمجرد انقطاع دم آخر حيض العدة إن كان الحيض عشرة أيام ؛ إذ الحيض لا مزيد له على عشرة، فإنّ ما زادَ عليها استحاضة على ما مر في موضعه. فبمجرد الانقطاع تخرج من الحيض فتنقضي العدّة وتنقطع الرجعة، وفيما دون العشرة يحتمل عود الحيض، فلا بد أن يتأكد الانقطاع بحقيقة الاغتسال أو بلزوم حكم من أحكام الطاهرات بمضي وقت الصلاة. كذا في «الهداية».
And if the blood of the last idda stops for ten days, it (the idda) is complete; and if less than that, it is not (complete) until she performs ghusl or the time for a prescribed prayer passes. His saying: 'Blood of the last idda' – it is the second menstruation in the slave woman, and the third in the free woman. For the idda of a free woman is three quru', and the idda of a slave woman is two menstruations. The upshot is that raj'a ends with the completion of the idda, and that occurs simply by the cessation of the blood of the last menstrual period of the idda if the menstruation lasted ten days; for menstruation does not exceed ten days, and what exceeds that is istihada, as previously stated in its place. So with mere cessation, she leaves menstruation and thus the idda expires and raj'a ends. For less than ten days, it is possible the menstruation may return, so it is essential that the cessation be confirmed by actual ghusl or by the application of a ruling of purity through the passage of prayer time. Thus in 'al-Hidaya'.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage provides a clear ruling on when the idda (waiting period after divorce) actually ends with respect to the last menstrual bleeding. It distinguishes between two situations:

1. If the blood of the last menstrual period (which is the third for a free woman, second for a slave woman) stops after exactly ten days, then the idda ends immediately upon cessation. This is because the maximum duration of menstrual blood (hayd) is ten days in the Hanafi school. Once ten days are completed, the blood that stops is definitely the end of hayd; any further bleeding beyond ten days would be istihada (chronic discharge), not menstrual. Therefore, the woman becomes pure instantly, and the idda is complete.

2. If the blood stops before ten days (e.g., after 7 days), the idda does not end immediately. This is because it is possible that the blood might return within the ten-day window. To be certain that the period has ended, there must be a sign of purity. Two signs are valid: (a) she performs ghusl (ritual bath) after the blood stops, or (b) the time for a fard prayer passes after the blood stops without her having performed ghusl. For example, if her blood stops at noon and she does not ghusl until the time for Zuhr prayer ends, the passing of the prayer time indicates that she is considered pure, and thus the idda ends at that moment.

The commentary clarifies that the 'last idda blood' refers to the final menstruation in the series of quru' (menstrual cycles) that constitute the idda. For a free woman, idda is three quru', so the last is the third menstruation; for a slave woman, it is the second. Once this last period ends with certainty (via the ten-day rule or confirmation after less), the idda is complete, and the husband's right of raj'a (revocation of divorce) is cut off.

Key Terms
دم آخر العدة (dam akhar al-idda): The blood of the last menstrual period that completes the idda. For a free woman, it is the third menstruation; for a slave woman, the second.
قرء (qur' (plural quru')): A menstrual cycle; in the Hanafi school, it is the period of menstruation plus the subsequent purity (tuhr) until the next menstruation.
استحاضة (istihada): Chronic non-menstrual bleeding that occurs outside the normal menstrual period, often due to illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon cessation.
B) After she performs ghusl or the time for a fard prayer passes.
C) After ten days from the start of bleeding.
D) Only after she has a third menstrual period.

Key Takeaway
The idda ends immediately only if the last menstrual blood stops at the full ten days of hayd; if it stops earlier, it must be confirmed by ghusl or the passing of a fard prayer time.

Whose Word Prevails? Wife's Claim of Idda Completion in Raj'a
intra-Hanafi dispute on wife's word in idda and raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding the acceptance of the wife's claim that her idda has ended at the time of raj'a and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's word; the Sahibayn accept the husband's.

When a husband attempts to revoke a revocable divorce (raj'a) and the wife claims her idda has already ended, a dispute arises over whose statement is legally accepted. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, focusing on the rationale behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The source presents two situations: (1) the husband simply believes or disbelieves the wife's claim; (2) the husband initiates raj'a and the wife immediately responds that her idda has passed, both denying.
The first scenario involves a slave woman and her master; the second involves a slave woman and her husband.
2
Recognize the agreed case
When the master believes the slave woman that her idda is over, his word is accepted by consensus among Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
This is a settled point; the ikhtilaf only arises when the master denies.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the idda is considered already complete at the moment the husband informs the master, so the master's right to mut'ah is established. The slave woman's statement would invalidate that right without evidence, so her word is not accepted.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument based on the assumption that the husband informed after the idda.
4
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold that the husband's or master's word is accepted over the wife's claim, likely based on general principles of evidence (e.g., the one claiming an exception bears the burden of proof).
The text does not elaborate their dalil, but it implies a default acceptance of the husband's statement in matters of marriage.
5
Apply the practical consequence
If the wife's word is accepted (Abu Hanifa), the raj'a is invalid because the idda has ended. If the husband's word is accepted (Sahibayn), the raj'a stands valid.
This thamarah al-khilaf directly affects the marital status of the couple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصدقه وكذبته، أو قال: راجعتك فقالت : مضت عدتي وأنكرا. [١] قوله : فصدقه؛ ولو كذبه المولى وصدقته الأمة فالقول للمولى اتفاقاً، وقيل: هو على الخلاف أيضاً، والصحيح أنه اتفاقي، والفرقُ لأبي حنيفة له أنّ الأمة منقضية العدّة في الحال؛ لأنّ الفرض أنّ الزوج أخبره بعدها، فثبت حق المولى في ملك المتعة، فلا يقبل قولها في إبطاله، بخلاف الوجه الأوّل، فإنّ المولى بالتصديق في الرجعة مقرّ بقيام العدة عند الرجعة، ولا يظهر ملك المولى مع العدة. كذا في «الهداية»(1). [٢]قوله : فقالت : يعني قال زوج الأمة : راجعتك مريداً به إنشاء الرجعة، فقالت فوراً : مضت عدتي، وأنكر الزوج وسيّد الأمة مضي العدة، فالقول قولها عنده لما مرّ.
He believed her and she disbelieved him, or he said: 'I have revoked you' and she said: 'My idda has passed' and they denied. [1] His statement: 'He believed her'; even if the master denied and the slave woman affirmed him, the word is the master's by consensus, and it is said it is also upon the difference, and the correct is that it is by consensus, and the distinction for Abu Hanifa is that the slave woman's idda is completed instantly because the assumption is that the husband informed him after it, so the master's right to ownership of mut'ah is established, so her statement to invalidate it is not accepted, contrary to the first aspect, for the master by believing in the raj'a is affirming the existence of the idda at the time of raj'a, and the master's ownership does not appear with the idda. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'She said' means the husband of the slave woman said: 'I have revoked you' intending thereby to initiate raj'a, and she immediately said: 'My idda has passed', and the husband and the master of the slave woman denied the passing of the idda, so the word is hers according to him (Abu Hanifa) for what has passed.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of raj'a when the wife claims her idda has ended. The matn states two scenarios: first, if the husband believes or disbelieves the wife's claim, and second, if the husband initiates raj'a and the wife immediately says her idda is over, both denying. The commentary clarifies that the first scenario concerns a slave woman and her master: if the master believes the slave woman that her idda is over, his word is accepted by consensus; the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) only arises if the master denies. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave woman's word is accepted because her idda is already complete at the time of the husband's informing, so the master's right to ownership is established. The two companions differ. The second scenario is where the husband of a slave woman says 'I have revoked you' and she immediately claims idda is over; Abu Hanifa accepts her word. The commentary cites al-Hidayah as the source.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) by the husband during the wife's idda, which restores the marriage without a new contract.
العدة (al-idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
قول (qawl): The statement or word of a party that is given weight in legal disputes, often a principle in judicial evidence (al-qawl li-man...).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the position of Abu Hanifa when a husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife immediately says 'My idda has passed'?
A) The husband's word is accepted because he is the one initiating raj'a.
B) The wife's word is accepted because the idda is considered complete at that moment.
C) The master's word is accepted if the wife is a slave.
D) The case is decided by drawing lots.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's claim that her idda has ended at the moment of raj'a, invalidating the revocation, while the Sahibayn uphold the husband's word and the raj'a's validity.

Completion of Idda Based on Cessation of Menstrual Blood
Ruling on completion of idda with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for completing idda: state the conditions under which idda ends immediately upon cessation of last menstrual blood versus requiring ghusl or the passing of prayer time.

Core Idea
If the last menstrual blood stops after ten days, idda ends immediately; if before ten days, idda ends only after ghusl or passing of prayer time.

The completion of idda is critical because it determines whether raj'a (taking back the wife) is still valid. This passage from al-Hidayah specifies the precise moment when idda ends for a woman observing her last menstruation. It distinguishes between a full ten-day menstrual period and a shorter cessation, where additional confirmation of purity is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand that the ruling applies to the last menstruation of idda: for a free woman it is the third menstruation; for a slave woman, the second.
2
Note the maximum menstruation rule
Recall that in the Hanafi school, menstruation lasts a minimum of 3 days and a maximum of 10 days. Beyond 10 days is istihadah.
This is a fundamental principle derived from prophetic tradition.
3
Apply the cessation rule for exactly 10 days
If the blood stops on the 10th day, the idda ends immediately because the cessation is certain (no possibility of return). Raj'a becomes invalid.
4
Apply the cessation rule for less than 10 days
If the blood stops before 10 days (e.g., on day 8), the idda is not complete until she performs ghusl or the time for an obligatory prayer passes. This confirms that the blood will not return.
The passing of prayer time makes prayer a debt on her, confirming purity.
5
Understand the rationale
The difference is due to possibility: after 10 days no return is possible; before 10 days, blood might resume, so a stronger sign of purity is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن انقطع دم آخرِ العدةِ لعشرة أيام تمت ولأقل منها لا، حتى تغتسل، أو يمضي عليها وقت فرض [١] قوله : دم آخر العدة؛ وهو الحيضُ الثاني في الأمة، والثالث في الحرة، فإنّ عدة الحرة ثلاثة قروء، وعدّة الأمة حيضتان، والحاصل أن الرجعة تنقطع بتمام العدة، وهو يكون بمجرد انقطاع دم آخر حيض العدة إن كان الحيض عشرة أيام؛ إذ الحيض لا مزيد له على عشرة، فإنّ ما زادَ عليها استحاضة على ما مر في موضعه. فبمجرد الانقطاع تخرج من الحيض فتنقضي العدّة وتنقطع الرجعة، وفيما دون العشرة يحتمل عود الحيض، فلا بد أن يتأكد الانقطاع بحقيقة الاغتسال أو بلزوم حكم من أحكام الطاهرات بمضي وقت الصلاة. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). (۱) إذ بمضي وقته صارت الصلاة ديناً في ذمتها، وهو قدر ما يقدر على الاغتسال والتحريمة وما دون ذلك ملحق بمدة الحيض. ينظر : (مجمع الأنهر» (١ : ٤٣٥). (۲) «الهداية» (٤ : ١٦٥ – ١٦٦). (۳) «الهداية» (٤) : ١٦٦).
And if the blood of the last (menstruation) of the idda stops for ten days, it (the idda) is complete; and if for less than that, it is not complete until she performs ghusl, or the time of an obligatory prayer passes upon her. [1] His statement: 'the blood of the last idda' – it is the second menstruation for a slave woman, and the third for a free woman, for the idda of a free woman is three menstrual periods, and the idda of a slave woman is two menstruations. The summary is that raj'a is cut off by the completion of the idda, and that happens merely by the cessation of the blood of the last menstruation of the idda if the menstruation was ten days, for menstruation has no increase beyond ten, for what exceeds it is istihadah (irregular bleeding) as has passed in its place. So by mere cessation she leaves menstruation and the idda ends and raj'a is cut off. In what is less than ten, the return of menstruation is possible, so the cessation must be confirmed by actual ghusl or by the application of a ruling of the pure women through the passing of the time of prayer. Thus in al-Hidayah. (1) Because with the passing of its time, prayer becomes a debt upon her, and it is the amount she is able to perform ghusl and takbirat al-ihram, and what is less than that is annexed to the duration of menstruation. Refer to Majma' al-Anhur (1:435). (2) al-Hidayah (4:165-166). (3) al-Hidayah (4:166).
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling on the precise moment when the idda ends for a woman in her last menstruation. The matn states that if the blood stops after a full ten days, the idda is complete immediately upon cessation. If it stops before ten days (e.g., after eight or nine days), the idda is not complete until she performs ghusl (ritual bath) or until the time for an obligatory prayer passes, which would indicate that she is pure. The commentary clarifies that the 'last blood' refers to the third menstruation for a free woman and the second for a slave woman, as per the Hanafi rule. The reasoning is that menstruation cannot exceed ten days; any bleeding beyond that is considered istihadah. If the bleeding lasts exactly ten days, cessation at the end of the tenth day is certain. If it stops earlier, there is a possibility it might return, so a further sign of purity is required: either actual ghusl or the passing of a prayer time without bleeding. The commentary cites al-Hidayah and notes that the passing of a prayer time makes prayer obligatory (a debt) and thus is considered a confirmation of purity.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood that lasts a minimum of three days and a maximum of ten days, after which it is considered istihadah.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular bleeding beyond the menstrual period (more than 10 days), which does not prevent fasting or prayer.
الاغتسال (al-ghusl): The ritual full-body wash that confirms purity after menstruation ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 10 days is the minimum for menstruation.
B) Because after 10 days, menstruation cannot return, so cessation is certain.
C) Because the woman is required to perform ghusl on the 10th day.
D) Because the idda of a free woman is exactly 10 days.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between cessation of menstrual blood at exactly ten days (immediate end of idda) and cessation before ten days (requires ghusl or prayer time for confirmation) based on the maximum length of menstruation.

Raj'a Permitted Despite Denial of Intercourse for Pregnant Wife
Ruling on raj'a when husband denies paternity of pregnant wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of raj'a when a husband denies intercourse with his pregnant wife and identify the legal principle that supports this ruling.

Core Idea
A husband's denial of paternity does not block his right to raj'a; the child is legally his by the marriage bed.

Can a man who divorces his pregnant wife and insists he never had relations with her still take her back? Classical Hanafi law says yes. This lesson examines the ruling and the powerful principle of al-walad li al-firash that governs it.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
Identify the key elements: a man divorces his wife who is pregnant, and he denies having had intercourse with her.
The denial is about the act of intercourse, not necessarily about paternity.
2
Identify the ruling
The text states that he has the right of raj'a (revocable divorce revocation) despite the denial.
Raj'a is only possible during the idda period, which for a pregnant woman ends at delivery.
3
Understand the basis
The legal principle 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the marriage bed) attributes the child to the husband, overriding his denial.
This principle is based on a hadith and is a cornerstone of family law in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Recognize the consequence
The husband's denial is legally irrelevant for the validity of raj'a; he cannot use it to avoid the revocation.
The law presumes the child is his unless there is a formal li'an (oath of condemnation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طلق حاملاً، أو مَن ولدَتْ مُنْكِراً وطأها، فله الرّجعة [٣]قوله : أو من ولدت ; أي ولدت الزوجة، ثم طلقها وقال : لم أجامعها أبداً. [٤]قوله : منكراً ; حال من فاعل طلق، ومتعلّق بكلتي الصورتين. [0]قوله : أقول ... الخ ; إيراد على المصنف الله بأن عبارته لا تفيد مقصوده بل (۱) أي له امرأة حامل طلقها وأنكر وطأها ثم راجعها ثم ولدت لأقل مدة الحمل من وقت النكاح صحت رجعته ولا عبرة بإنكاره للوطء ; لأن الشرع كذبه بجعل الولد للفراش. ينظر : درر الحكام»(١ : ٣٨٥). (۲) «الهداية» (٤ : ١٦٧).
And if he divorces a pregnant woman, or one who gave birth, while denying intercourse with her, he has the right of raj'a. [3] His statement: 'or one who gave birth' means the wife gave birth, then he divorced her and said: 'I never had intercourse with her.' [4] His statement: 'denying' is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'divorced', and it pertains to both scenarios. [0] His statement: 'I say... etc.' is an objection to the author that his expression does not convey his intended meaning, but (1) meaning he has a pregnant wife, he divorced her and denied intercourse, then revoked her, then she gave birth in less than the minimum duration of pregnancy from the time of marriage, his raj'a is valid and his denial of intercourse is disregarded because the law belies him by attributing the child to the marriage bed. See Durar al-Hukkam (1:385). (2) al-Hidayah (4:167).
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a man divorces his wife while she is pregnant, or after she has given birth, and he denies that he ever had intercourse with her (i.e., he denies paternity), he still has the right to perform raj'a (revocation of the divorce). The commentary explains that 'denying intercourse' is a description of the husband's state at the time of divorce, and it applies to both scenarios (pregnant or already delivered). The commentator then objects that the author's wording is insufficient to convey the intended meaning. The footnote clarifies the complete ruling: if a man divorces his pregnant wife, denies intercourse, then revokes (raj'a), and she gives birth within the minimum duration of pregnancy from the time of marriage (less than 6 months), the raj'a is valid and his denial is not accepted because Islamic law attributes the child to the marriage bed (فراش), so the child is considered his. The footnote cites Durar al-Hukkam and al-Hidayah as sources.

Key Terms
فراش (firash): The marital bed, used in the principle 'al-walad li al-firash' meaning a child born under a valid marriage is attributed to the husband.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The revocation of a revocable divorce during the idda period, which restores the marriage without a new contract.
الإنكار (al-inkar): Denial, specifically the husband's denial of having had intercourse with his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He can still perform raj'a because the child is legally attributed to him.
D) He must wait until the child is born to decide.

Key Takeaway
The principle of al-walad li al-firash overrides a husband's denial of intercourse, ensuring the validity of raj'a and protecting the child's lineage.

Revocation When the Husband Denies Intercourse with the Pregnant Wife
Commentator resolves an intra-madhhab contradiction with a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition for revocation when the husband denies intercourse and apply it to scenarios with different birth timings.

Core Idea
Revocation is only valid if the wife gives birth within six months, proving the husband's denial false.

The apparent ruling in the matn seems unconditional: a husband can revoke a divorced pregnant wife even if he denies intercourse. But the commentator raises a sharp objection: how can revocation exist without an 'iddah? This lesson dissects the reasoning and the condition that resolves the contradiction, showing how Hanafi jurists prioritize certainty over mere claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Apparent Contradiction
Read the matn's ruling: the man divorces a pregnant wife and denies intercourse; the matn says he has the right of revocation. The commentator objects: denial implies no consummation, hence no 'iddah, so revocation should be impossible. Identify the gap between the ruling and the underlying principles.
The denial is not just about intercourse but about paternity: he denies the pregnancy is from him.
2
Locate the Commentator's Resolution
The commentator says the husband's denial is only disregarded when his falsity is proven by Shari'ah. That happens only if she gives birth within six months of the divorce (since the minimum pregnancy term is six months). Thus, revocation is valid only in that window—before delivery but after the six-month mark? Actually, before delivery (since 'iddah ends at delivery) but the condition for validity is that the birth must occur within six months from divorce. Trace the logic: if birth < 6 months, his denial is false, so consummation is confirmed, 'iddah exists, revocation valid before birth.
Six months = minimum duration of pregnancy in Hanafi law (based on Quran 46:15 and hadith).
3
Analyze the First Objection from al-Bahr
Al-Bahr objects: pregnancy can be established before birth by visible signs; why wait for birth? The response: the husband's admission of no intercourse creates a conflicting evidence; only a birth within six months provides certainty (qat') of falsity. Signs of pregnancy give only probable knowledge (ghalabat al-zann), which is insufficient to override his claim.
This highlights the Hanafi preference for certainty over probability when a contradictory claim exists.
4
Consider the Second Objection and Its Implications
The second objection: if he revokes and she later gives birth within two years, lineage is established, showing pregnancy can exceed six months. This objection is not fully answered here, but it leads to a nuance: the six-month condition applies only to the initial dispute; if he revokes and later birth occurs, lineage follows the bed (al-walad li-l-firash). The key takeaway: the six-month rule is specific to proving the husband's falsity.
The passage ends without a complete rebuttal, indicating the complexity of the matter.
5
Synthesize the Final Ruling
The mufta bihi position: revocation is valid only if the wife gives birth within six months of the divorce. If she gives birth at six months or later, revocation is never valid (neither before nor after birth). If she gives birth before six months, he can revoke before delivery.
This condition is a later refinement (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not explicit in the early texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أو من ولدت ؛ أي ولدت الزوجة، ثم طلقها وقال : لم أجامعها أبداً.
[٤]قوله : منكراً ؛ حال من فاعل طلق، ومتعلّق بكلتي الصورتين. [0]قوله : أقول ... الخ ؛ إيراد على المصنف الله بأن عبارته لا تفيد مقصوده بل مختلة ؛ وذلك لأنه لَمَّا طَلّق زوجته وهي حامل وأنكر وطأها بأن قال : لم أجامعها قط، وتضمّن هذا الإنكار إنكار كون حملها منه ، فبمقتضى زعمه لا تصح الرجعة ؛ لأن الرجعة إنّما تكون في العدّة ولا عدة لغير المدخول بها.
وقول المصنف له في هذه الصورة: فله الرجعة، يفيد أنه تجوز له الرجعة ، ولا يعتبر زعمه وهو مشكل، فإن عدم اعتبار زعمه إنما يكون إذا ظهر كذبه شرعاً، وهو لا يكون إلا إذا وضعت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق، فلا تصح له الرجعة قبله ، وأما بعـده فلا تصح أيضاً ؛ لانقضائها، فإنّ الحامل عدتها وضع حملها، وبالجملة لا تستقيم صحة الرجعة لا قبل وضع الحمل ولا بعده.
(1)

والحاصل أن معرفة كونها ذات حمل عند الطلاق لا يتوقف على الولادة، فيمكن الحكم بصحة الرجعة قبلها ، بناءً على أنه صار مُكذِّباً في قوله: «لم أجامعها» ؛ لظهور الحمل، وكون عدة ذات حمل وضع حملها.
وثانيهما: إنه سيجيء أنه لو راجعها ثم ولدت لأقل من عامين يثبت نسبه، فعلم به أن الحمل يعرف بالولادة لأكثر من ستة أشهر أيضاً.
وأجيب عن الأوّل: بأن ثبوت الحمل بظهور أماراته بحيث يغلب ظنّ كلّ من شاهدها بكونها حاملاً ، إنما يكون إذا لم يعارضه غيره، وقد عارضه غيره في مسألتنا ، فإنّ إقراره بأنه لم يطأ ينافي صحة رجعته ما لم يظهر كذبه قطعاً، وذلك لا يكون إلا بأن تلد لأقل من ستة أشهر.
His statement: "Or if she gives birth" – meaning the wife gave birth, then he divorced her and said: I never had intercourse with her. [4] His statement: "denying" – is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of "divorced", and it relates to both scenarios. [0] His statement: "I say... etc." – an objection against the author (may Allah have mercy on him) that his expression does not convey his intended meaning but is defective. This is because when he divorced his pregnant wife and denied intercourse with her by saying: I never had intercourse with her at all, this denial implies denial that the pregnancy is from him. According to his claim, revocation is not valid because revocation only occurs during the 'iddah, and there is no 'iddah for a woman who has not been consummated with. The author's statement in this case: "He has the right of revocation" indicates that revocation is permissible for him, and his claim is not considered. This is problematic because his claim is not considered only when his falsity is evident by Shari'ah, and that occurs only if she gives birth at less than six months from the time of divorce. So revocation is not valid before that, and after that it is also not valid because her 'iddah has ended, since the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is delivery of her pregnancy. In summary, the validity of revocation is not established either before or after delivery.
(1) [11] His statement: "It is known only... etc." – The author of al-Bahr and others objected to him with two points. First: The existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is not dependent on what was mentioned; rather, pregnancy may be established before delivery, and lineage is established by it as they stated in the chapter on option of defect: The pregnancy of a sold slave girl is established by its appearance before delivery, and in the chapter on establishing lineage: It is established by apparent pregnancy. The conclusion is that knowing she is pregnant at the time of divorce is not dependent on delivery, so it is possible to rule on the validity of revocation before it, based on the fact that he is disproven in his statement "I did not have intercourse with her" due to the appearance of pregnancy, and the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is delivery. Second: It will come later that if he revokes her and then she gives birth within less than two years, his lineage is established. Thus it is known that pregnancy is recognized by delivery after more than six months as well. He responded to the first: The establishment of pregnancy by the appearance of its signs such that every observer strongly thinks she is pregnant only occurs when there is no conflicting evidence. But in our issue, there is conflicting evidence: his admission that he did not have intercourse contradicts the validity of his revocation unless his falsity is absolutely proven, and that only occurs by her giving birth at less than six months.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a commentary and critical refinement on a ruling in the matn: "If he divorces a pregnant woman, or one who gives birth while denying his intercourse, he has the right of revocation." The commentator raises an objection: the man's denial of intercourse implies no consummation, hence no 'iddah, so how can there be revocation? He resolves it by stating that the man's denial is only disregarded when proven false by Shari'ah, which happens if she gives birth within six months of divorce (since the minimum pregnancy term is six months). Thus, revocation is valid only in that window. The commentary then presents two objections from 'al-Bahr' (a later Hanafi work). First objection: pregnancy can be established before delivery by signs, so why wait for birth? Second objection: if she gives birth within two years after revocation, lineage is established, showing pregnancy can be longer than six months. The response to the first objection: the signs of pregnancy are overridden by the husband's admission of no intercourse; only a birth at less than six months provides certainty of falsity. The second objection is not fully answered here, but the point is that the ruling stands on the principle of certainty.

Key Terms
رجعة (ruj'ah): Revocation of divorce by the husband during the 'iddah period.
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce or death of husband during which a woman cannot remarry.
وطء (watʾ): Sexual intercourse; here it refers to consummation of the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man divorces his pregnant wife and declares he never had intercourse with her. She gives birth exactly 5 months after the divorce. According to the Hanafi position in this passage, is the husband's revocation valid?
A) Yes, because his denial is disproven by birth at less than six months.
B) No, because his denial is always taken as true.
C) Yes, because any pregnancy proves his denial false.
D) No, because she gave birth after the 'iddah ended.

Key Takeaway
Revocation by a husband who denies intercourse is only valid if the wife gives birth within six months of the divorce, thereby proving his denial false.

Revocation of Divorce for a Pregnant Wife with Denied Intercourse
Resolving conflict between husband's denial and apparent pregnancy for revocation validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on revocation of divorce when the husband denies intercourse with a pregnant wife, and evaluate the condition of birth within six months as proof of lying.

Core Idea
A husband's denial of intercourse blocks revocation until the wife gives birth within six months, proving his lie.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a subtle problem: a husband divorces his pregnant wife but claims he never had intercourse with her. How can revocation be valid if, by his own admission, there is no 'iddah? The commentator resolves this by showing that revocation is only validated retroactively if the wife gives birth within six months of the divorce. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the ruling: the author (al-Marghinani) says 'he has the right of revocation'. Determine what this means in light of the husband's denial.
The ruling appears paradoxical because denial implies no 'iddah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the Quranic evidence: Surah Al-Talaq 65:4, which states that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery.
This verse is key for determining 'iddah duration.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the verse leads to the six-month condition: If the wife gives birth within six months of divorce, it proves the pregnancy existed at divorce, so 'iddah existed, and revocation is valid retroactively.
Six months is the minimum viable pregnancy duration from conception to birth.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Examine the objection from al-Bahr: that pregnancy can be known by appearance before birth, so revocation should be valid immediately.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi difference.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Analyze the commentator's response: the husband's admission contradicts the appearance; only categorical proof (birth within six months) overcomes his denial.
The relied-upon position requires certainty.

كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة
لأن وجود الحمل وقت الطلاق إنما يعرف إذا ولدت هي : مختلة ؛ وذلك لأنه لما طلق زوجته وهي حامل وأنكر وطأها بأن قال : لم أجامعها قط، وتضمن هذا الإنكار إنكار كون حملها منه ، فبمقتضى زعمه لا تصح الرجعة ؛ لأن الرجعة إنما تكون في العدة ولا عدة لغير المدخول بها.
وقول المصنف له في هذه الصورة: فله الرجعة، يفيد أنه تجوز له الرجعة ، ولا يعتبر زعمه وهو مشكل، فإن عدم اعتبار زعمه إنما يكون إذا ظهر كذبه شرعاً، وهو لا يكون إلا إذا وضعت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق، فلا تصح له الرجعة قبله ، وأما بعده فلا تصح أيضاً ؛ لانقضائها، فإن الحامل عدتها وضع حملها، وبالجملة لا تستقيم صحة الرجعة لا قبل وضع الحمل ولا بعده.
(1)
11 ] قوله : إنما يعرف ... الخ ؛ أورد عليه صاحب «البحر» وغيره بوجهين : أحدهما : بأن ثبوت الحمل عند الطلاق غير موقوف على ما ذكر، بل قد يثبت الحمل قبل الوضع، ويثبت به النسب لما صرحوا به في باب خيار العيب : إن حمل الجارية المبيعة يثبت بظهوره قبل الوضع، وفي باب ثبوت النسب : إنه يثبت بالحبل الظاهر.
والحاصل أن معرفة كونها ذات حمل عند الطلاق لا يتوقف على الولادة، فيمكن الحكم بصحة الرجعة قبلها ، بناءً على أنه صار مكذبا في قوله: «لم أجامعها» ؛ لظهور الحمل، وكون عدة ذات حمل وضع حملها.
وثانيهما: إنه سيجيء أنه لو راجعها ثم ولدت لأقل من عامين يثبت نسبه، فعلم به أن الحمل يعرف بالولادة لأكثر من ستة أشهر أيضاً.
وأجيب عن الأوّل: بأن ثبوت الحمل بظهور أماراته بحيث يغلب ظن كل من شاهدها بكونها حاملاً ، إنما يكون إذا لم يعارضه غير، وقد عارضه غيره في مسألتنا ، فإن إقراره بأنه لم يطأ ينافي صحة رجعته ما لم يظهر كذبه قطعاً، وذلك لا يكون إلا بأن تلد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق ، فإذا ولدت انقضت العدة، فلا يملك الرجعة، فيكون المراد بالرجعة الرجعة قبل وضع الحمل، فيكون المراد أنه إن راجع قبل وضع الحمل، فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر يحكم بصحة الرجعة السابقة، ولا يراد أنه يحل له الرجعة قبل وضع الحمل.
وقال بعضهم في الجواب عنه : بمنع اعتبار ثبوت الحمل بظهوره، وفيه ضعف كما بسطه في رد المحتار على الدر المختار).
والجواب عن الثاني : إن الطلاق في المسألة الآتية مفروض بعد إقراره بالخلوة بها ، والطلاق بعد الخلوة موجب للعدة ، ومعتدة الرجعي إذا لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها وجاءت بولد ثبت نسبه، لكن إن ولدته لأكثر من سنتين كانت الولادة رجعة، وإلا لا ؛ لجواز علوقه قبل الطلاق، فإذا ثبت نسبه وكان قد راجعها بالقول مثلا تبينت صحة تلك الرجعة بالولادة لأقل من عامين.
أما في هذه المسألة فإنه لم يقر بالخلوة، لتلزمها العدة، فإذا طلقها يكون طلاقاً قبل الدخول ظاهراً، فلا عدة عليها ، فإذا ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر تبين أن الطلاق كان بعد الدخول وأنها معتدة ، فإذا كان قد راجعها قبل الولادة تبينت صحة الرجعة ؛ لكونها في العدة.
[1]قوله : لأقل ... الخ ؛ أما إذا ولدت بعد ستة أشهر أو لتمام ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق فلا يعرف وجود الحمل قطعاً عند الطلاق، فلا تصح الرجعة السابقة.
[2]قوله : انقضت العدة ؛ لقوله : وَأُولَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ (2)
[3]قوله : فيكون ؛ تفريع على ما ذكره من وجه التساهل، وتوجيه لكلام المصنف ه ، وحاصله : أنه لما علم أنه لا يمكن حمل قوله : فله الرجعة على صحة الرجعة بعد الولادة أو قبلها، فيكون المراد من قوله : فله الرجعة أنه لو راجع قبل الولادة يحكم بصحته الآن إذا ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر ؛ لأنه يظهر به كذبه في قوله : لم أجامعها ، ويعلم أنها كانت معتدة بالطلاق بعد الوطء وأن رجعته وقعت في العدة.
(1) رد المحتار (3: 405-406).
(2) الطلاق : من الآية 4.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation (Raj'a). Because the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is only known when she gives birth. This is problematic; because when he divorced his wife while she was pregnant and denied having intercourse with her by saying: 'I never had intercourse with her at all,' and this denial includes the denial that her pregnancy is from him, then according to his claim the revocation is not valid; because revocation only occurs during the 'iddah, and there is no 'iddah for one who has not been entered upon. And the author's statement regarding this scenario: 'he has the right of revocation' indicates that revocation is permissible for him, and his claim is not considered. This is problematic, for his claim is disregarded only if he is shown to be lying by Shar'i means, and that only occurs if she gives birth at less than six months from the time of divorce. So revocation is not valid for him before that, and also not after it because the 'iddah has ended; for the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is the delivery of her child. In summary, the validity of revocation is not correct either before delivery or after it. (1) [Footnote: Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 4:59] 11. His statement: 'It is only known... etc.'; the author of al-Bahr and others raised two objections against it. First: The confirmation of pregnancy at the time of divorce is not dependent on what was mentioned; rather, pregnancy may be confirmed before delivery, and lineage is thereby established, as they explicitly stated in the chapter on the option of defect: the pregnancy of a sold slave girl is established by its appearance before delivery, and in the chapter on establishing lineage: it is established by apparent pregnancy. The gist is that knowing that she is pregnant at the time of divorce is not dependent on delivery; so one can judge the validity of revocation before it, based on the fact that he is proven a liar in his statement 'I never had intercourse with her' due to the appearance of pregnancy, and that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is her delivery. Second: It will come later that if he revoked and then she gave birth within two years, his lineage is established; so one knows from this that pregnancy is also known by delivery after more than six months. The answer to the first: The establishment of pregnancy by the appearance of its signs, to the extent that every observer strongly believes she is pregnant, is only valid when there is no contradictory factor. In our case, there is a contradictory factor: his admission that he did not have intercourse contradicts the validity of his revocation unless his lying is definitively shown, and that only occurs by her giving birth in less than six months from the time of divorce. If she gives birth, the 'iddah ends, so he does not own the right of revocation. Thus the intended meaning of revocation is revocation before delivery, meaning that if he revoked before delivery and she gave birth at less than six months, the previous revocation is judged valid. It is not intended that revocation is permissible for him before delivery. Some scholars answered by denying the validity of establishing pregnancy by its appearance, but this is weak, as detailed in Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The answer to the second: The divorce in the upcoming case is premised on his admission of seclusion (khalwa) with her, and divorce after seclusion necessitates 'iddah. If a woman in revocable 'iddah does not admit its expiry and brings a child, his lineage is established. However, if she gives birth after more than two years, the birth is considered a revocation, otherwise not; because conception could have occurred before divorce. So if lineage is established and he had revoked verbally, the validity of that revocation becomes clear through birth within less than two years. In our case, he did not admit seclusion, so 'iddah is not incumbent. So when he divorces her, it is apparently a divorce before consummation, so there is no 'iddah. So if she gives birth in less than six months, it becomes clear that the divorce was after consummation and she is in 'iddah. So if he had revoked before birth, the validity of the revocation becomes clear because it occurred during the 'iddah. [1] His statement: 'less than... etc.': If she gives birth after six months or at exactly six months from the time of divorce, the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is not known definitively, so the previous revocation is not valid. [2] His statement: 'the 'iddah ends': based on His saying: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their burden' (Quran 65:4). [3] His statement: 'thus it becomes' is a branch on what he mentioned regarding the aspect of concession, and an interpretation of the author's statement. Its gist: Since it is known that his statement 'he has the right of revocation' cannot be taken to mean validity of revocation after delivery or before it, the intended meaning of his statement 'he has the right of revocation' is that if he revoked before delivery, it is now judged valid if she gives birth at less than six months; because his lying in his statement 'I never had intercourse with her' becomes apparent, and it is known that she was in 'iddah from divorce after intercourse and that his revocation occurred during the 'iddah.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah deals with a nuanced problem regarding revocation (رجعة) of divorce when the husband divorces a pregnant wife but denies having ever had intercourse with her. The author of the matn (المصنف) ruled that he has the right to revoke (فله الرجعة), but this seems problematic because if he denies intercourse, then according to his own claim there is no 'iddah (since 'iddah only applies after consummation), and thus no basis for revocation. The commentator (the author of Umda al-Ri'ayah) resolves this by explaining that the ruling is not absolute. The validity of the revocation depends on the wife giving birth less than six months from the date of divorce. If she does so, it proves that the husband's denial was false, that intercourse did occur, and that she was in 'iddah at the time of revocation. If she gives birth at six months or more, the pregnancy could have begun after divorce, so the husband's denial remains plausible, and the revocation is invalid. The commentator also addresses objections from al-Bahr and others: first, that pregnancy can be known before delivery, so why wait? The answer: here the husband's admission contradicts; we need definitive proof of lying, which only birth within six months provides. Second, that another passage seems to allow longer periods (up to two years) for lineage establishment. The answer: that passage deals with a different scenario where the husband admitted seclusion (خلوة), and the 'iddah is established from the start. Our case lacks such admission. Thus the ruling is consistent: in this specific scenario, revocation is only validated retroactively if birth occurs within six months of the divorce.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-raj'a): Revocation of a revocable divorce by the husband during the wife's 'iddah, without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.
الحمل (al-haml): Pregnancy; here specifically the condition of the wife at the time of divorce that affects 'iddah and revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario described, when is the revocation (رجعة) of a pregnant divorced wife valid if the husband denies having had intercourse?
A) Immediately upon appearance of pregnancy signs.
B) Only if the wife gives birth within six months of the divorce.
C) At any time before the wife gives birth, regardless of her denial.
D) Never, because if he denies intercourse, there is no 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a husband's denial of intercourse with his divorced pregnant wife blocks revocation until birth within six months proves his lie, ensuring 'iddah certainty.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Revocable Divorce Validated by Birth Within Six Months
Expanding on a ruling with proofs from Quran and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocable divorce when the husband denies intercourse and the wife gives birth within six months, identify the Quranic and hadith proofs, and explain the legal reasoning behind the validity of the raj'ah.

Core Idea
Birth within six months of divorce proves pregnancy at divorce, confirming the wife was in 'iddah and making any prior raj'ah valid.

This passage examines a specific scenario: a husband divorces his wife while denying ever having had intercourse with her. Later, before any birth, he revokes the divorce (raj'ah). Then the wife gives birth within six months of the divorce. Is the raj'ah valid? The Hanafi school rules that it is, because the birth proves she was pregnant at the time of divorce, meaning she was in 'iddah, and the revocation occurred during the 'iddah. The reasoning relies on the Quranic verse on the 'iddah of pregnant women and the hadith on lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A husband denies intercourse, divorces his wife, then revokes the divorce before any birth. The wife gives birth within six months of the divorce.
The denial of intercourse could suggest no 'iddah, but the birth changes the situation.
2
State the ruling
The earlier raj'ah is valid. The husband regains his wife without a new contract.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi).
3
Locate the Quranic proof
Quran 65:4: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden.' This shows that a pregnant woman's 'iddah ends with birth. The birth within six months proves pregnancy at divorce, so the woman was in 'iddah at the time of raj'ah.
The verse implies that if birth occurs within the pregnancy period, the woman was pregnant, hence in 'iddah.
4
Locate the hadith proof
The hadith 'The child belongs to the bed' (al-waladu lil-firash) establishes that the child's lineage is from the husband (the bed). By extension, the marriage is valid, and thus the 'iddah is valid.
This hadith, though ahad, is accepted in Hanafi school for this purpose.
5
Trace the logical chain
Birth within six months → pregnancy existed at divorce → divorce was after consummation → wife in 'iddah → raj'ah occurred during 'iddah → valid.
The key is that the birth retrospectively proves the situation.
6
Note the exception
If birth occurs after six months or at exactly six months, there is no certainty of pregnancy at divorce, so the raj'ah is not valid.
The six-month period is the minimum duration of pregnancy in Hanafi law.
7
Examine the commentator's refinement
The commentator clarifies that the author's expression could be misinterpreted. The correct phrasing is that if a man divorces his pregnant wife while denying intercourse, then revokes, and she gives birth in less than six months, the raj'ah is valid.
This avoids implying that raj'ah is permissible after birth, which would be incorrect.

For less than six months from the time of divorce. So when she gives birth, the waiting period ends, and he does not have the right of revocable divorce. So what is meant by revocable divorce is the revocable divorce before delivery. So the meaning is that if he revokes before delivery, and she gives birth in less than six months, it is judged that the earlier revocable divorce is valid, and it is not meant that revocable divorce is permissible for him before delivery. And some said in answer to it: by denying the establishment of pregnancy by its appearance, and there is weakness in it, as elaborated in Radd al-Muhtar on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And the answer to the second: that the divorce in the following issue is assumed after his acknowledgment of seclusion with her, and divorce after seclusion necessitates the waiting period. And the woman whose revocable divorce is valid, if she does not acknowledge the end of her waiting period and brings a child, his lineage is established. But if she gives birth after more than two years, the birth is a revocable divorce, otherwise not; because it is possible that conception occurred before the divorce. So if his lineage is established and he had revoked her verbally, for example, the validity of that revocable divorce becomes clear by birth within less than two years. As for this issue, he did not acknowledge seclusion, so the waiting period is not incumbent on her. So if he divorces her, it is apparently a divorce before consummation, so there is no waiting period on her. So if she gives birth in less than six months, it becomes clear that the divorce was after consummation and that she is in the waiting period. So if he had revoked before the birth, the validity of the revocable divorce becomes clear because it was during the waiting period. [1] His saying: 'for less than...': As for if she gives birth after six months or exactly six months from the time of divorce, the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce is not known with certainty, so the earlier revocable divorce is not valid. [2] His saying: 'the waiting period ends': due to His saying: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden' (Quran 65:4). [3] His saying: 'so it is': a branch on what he mentioned of the aspect of leniency, and a direction of the author's speech. Its summary: that when it is known that his saying 'he has the right of revocable divorce' cannot be applied to the validity of revocable divorce after birth or before it, so what is meant by his saying 'he has the right of revocable divorce' is that if he revoked before birth, it is judged valid now if she gives birth in less than six months; because by it his falsehood in his saying 'I did not have intercourse with her' becomes apparent, and it is known that she was in the waiting period by divorce after intercourse and that his revocable divorce occurred during the waiting period. Because when he denied intercourse, and the law does not judge the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce, but rather judges only if she gives birth in less than six months from the time of divorce, there was no legal refutation before delivery. So the correct expression is to say: And whoever divorces a pregnant woman while denying intercourse, then revokes her, and she brings a child in less than six months, the revocable divorce is valid. As for the issue of birth, its form is: that he divorces his wife who gave birth before the divorce while denying intercourse, so he has the right of revocable divorce. [1] His saying: 'when he denied...': meaning when he said 'I never had intercourse with this woman', according to his claim, the waiting period is not obligatory on her, so revocable divorce is not permissible for him. Yes, if she gives birth in less than six months, he becomes legally refuted, meaning the Lawgiver refutes him in his saying 'I did not have intercourse with her' due to the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce. And it has been reported in authentic traditions: 'The child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone.' [2] His saying: 'so the correct expression': It may be said: this necessitates that the author's speech is erroneous and mistaken, and the previous direction necessitates that it is correct with interpretation and leniency, so there is contradiction between the two statements of the commentator. The answer to it is that the use of 'correct expression' here is based on exaggeration, for the deficiency in understanding the intended meaning is close to error according to them. [3] His saying: 'that he divorces': meaning he divorced his wife saying that he did not have intercourse with her, and she had given birth before the divorce, so revocable divorce is permissible for him; because his saying that he did not have intercourse with her, although it necessitates that the marriage is not valid.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the validity of revocable divorce (رجعة) when the husband denies having had intercourse with his wife, but she gives birth within six months of the divorce. The commentator expands on the author's text, clarifying that if the wife gives birth less than six months after the divorce, it becomes evident that she was pregnant at the time of divorce, meaning she was in the 'iddah (waiting period). Therefore, any revocable divorce made by the husband before the birth is valid. The proof is the Quranic verse (65:4) that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman ends with birth, and the hadith 'الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر' which establishes lineage and implies the marriage was intact. The commentator also addresses objections: if the husband denied intercourse, but the birth within six months legally refutes his claim, as the child's lineage is established from the marriage. The correct expression, according to the commentator, is that if a man divorces his pregnant wife while denying intercourse, then revokes, and she gives birth within six months, the revocable divorce is valid. This ruling is part of the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocable divorce: the husband's right to take back his wife during her 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse; here it refers to consummation of the marriage.
حمل (haml): Pregnancy; the state of being pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if a husband divorces his wife while denying having had intercourse with her, and she later gives birth within six months of the divorce, what is the ruling on any revocable divorce (رجعة) he made before the birth?
A) The revocable divorce is invalid because he denied intercourse.
B) The revocable divorce is valid because the birth proves she was in 'iddah.
C) The revocable divorce is only valid if she gives birth after six months.
D) The revocable divorce is valid only if he acknowledges the child.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife's birth within six months of divorce retroactively proves she was in 'iddah, validating any revocable divorce made by the husband even if he denied intercourse.

Revocation of Divorce with Denial of Intercourse: Pregnancy and Khalwah
Explaining when revocation is valid despite denial of intercourse, using hadith to distinguish pregnancy/childbirth from khalwah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the scenarios of pregnancy/childbirth and seclusion (khalwah) and state the Hanafi ruling on raj'ah when the husband denies intercourse, citing the hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' as proof.

Core Idea
A husband's denial of intercourse is overridden by the birth of a child within six months, but not by mere seclusion.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a nuanced ruling in Hanafi family law: when a husband revokes a revocable divorce (raj'ah) while denying he ever had intercourse with his wife. The ruling turns on whether the denial is contradicted by clear Shar'i evidence. Two key scenarios are contrasted: pregnancy/childbirth and khalwah (seclusion). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the law to cases where paternity or 'iddah is contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two scenarios
Pinpoint the legal problem: a husband divorces and denies intercourse, so by his claim there is no 'iddah, thus no basis for raj'ah. The passage examines two situations: (1) the wife is pregnant or gives birth, (2) the husband had khalwah but denies intercourse.
2
Analyze the pregnancy/childbirth scenario
If the wife gives birth less than six months after divorce (or is pregnant at divorce), the birth/pregnancy contradicts the husband's denial. The hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' attributes the child to the husband, legally proving intercourse occurred. Therefore, 'iddah is established, and raj'ah becomes valid.
The key proof is the hadith, which is qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalalah for this ruling.
3
Analyze the khalwah scenario
If the husband had seclusion but denies actual intercourse, his denial is not legally refuted because khalwah does not necessitate intercourse. The law imposes 'iddah and full mahr for khalwah as a precaution, not as proof of intercourse. Hence, his denial stands, no 'iddah is proven, and raj'ah is invalid.
Contrast: khalwah triggers certain legal effects but does not make the husband a liar.
4
Critique of author's wording (intra-Hanafi refinement)
The commentator notes a rhetorical issue: the author's phrase 'so the correct thing' might imply the original text was wrong, but the commentator explains it is a matter of emphasis, not error. This shows how later scholars refine language without altering the ruling.
This step is optional for understanding the core ruling but shows scholarly method.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
Given a scenario, determine whether raj'ah is valid by checking if there is Shar'i evidence that overrides the husband's denial. The only overriding evidence in this chapter is childbirth within six months (or pregnancy). Khalwah alone does not suffice.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is whether the couple can resume marriage without a new contract.

Source Text

وأما مسألة الولادة فصورتها : أنه طلق امرأته التي وَلَدَت قبل الطلاق مُنْكِراً وطأها، فله الرّجعة
[قوله : لما أنكر ... الخ ؛ يعني لما قال : إني لم أجامع هذه أبداً تكون لا تجب عليها العدة حسب زعمه، فلا تحل له الرجعة، نعم إذا ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر صار مكذباً شرعاً ، يعني : إنّ الشارع كذَّبه في قوله : لم أجامعها ؛ لوجود الحـبـل عـنـد الطلاق، وقد ورد في الأخبار الصحيحة : «إنّ الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر»
[قوله : فالصواب ؛ قد يقال : هذا يقتضي أن يكون كلام المصنف خطأ غلطاً، وما سبق من التوجيه يقتضي أنه صحيح مع تأويل وتساهل، فبين كلامي الشارح تدافع. والجواب عنه : إن إطلاق الصواب هاهنا مبني على المبالغة، فإنّ الإخلال في فهم المقصود في قوة الخطأ عندهم.
[قوله : إنه طلق ؛ يعني : طلّق زوجته قائلاً إنّه لم يجامعها، وقد ولدت عنده قبل الطلاق، فتجوز له الرجعة ؛ لأنّ قوله : إنّه لم يجامعها وإن كان مقتضاه أن لا تصح العدة.
وإنما تصحُ الرَّجعة في مسألتي الحمل والولادة مع إنكاره الوطء ؛ لأنَّ الشَّرعَ كذَّبه في إنكاره الوطء ؛ لأنَّ الولد للفراش .
وان خلا بها وأنكر فلا : أي لا تصح رجعتها ؛ لأنه أنكر الوطء، ولم يوجد تكذيب الشرع إنكاره ، فيكون إنكاره حجة عليه .
[قوله : وإن خلا ؛ ماض من الخلوة، حاصله : إنه خلا بامرأته ثمَّ طلّقها منكراً وطأها لا تصح رجعته ؛ لأنّ المراد يؤخذ بإقراره ما لم يكذبه الشرع أو شهادة الوجود، وقد أقرّ على نفسه بأنه لم يطأها، والرجعه إنّما تصح في عدة المجامعة لا في عدّة الخلوة ، بخلاف ما مر من مسألتي الحمل والولادة، فإنّ الإقرار هناك بطل بوجود الحمل والولادة، ولا كذلك هاهنا.
فإن قلت : قد جعل الشرعُ الخلوة مقام الوطء ودليلاً على وجوده، حيث أوجب العدة على مَن طُلقت بعد الخلوة بدون الوطء، وأوجب المهر الكامل بعد الخلوة، فلم لم يجعل الإقرار باطلاً هاهنا بوجود الخلوة، وإقراره بها.

[قوله : حجة عليه ؛ حيث يبطل حق الرجعة وهو حقه، ولو أقرّ بالوطء
Book of Divorce/Chapter of Revocation. Because he denied intercourse, and the Law does not rule the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce, but rather rules when she gives birth at less than six months from the time of divorce, so there is no refutation of the Law before the placement of the pregnancy. So the correct thing is to say: And whoever divorces a pregnant woman while denying intercourse with her, and then revokes her, and she comes with a child at less than six months, the revocation is valid. As for the issue of childbirth, its form is: that he divorced his wife who had given birth before the divorce while denying intercourse with her, so he has revocation. [Commentary: 'Because he denied' etc.: meaning since he said 'I never had intercourse with this woman,' there is no 'iddah upon her according to his claim, so revocation is not permissible for him. Yes, if she gives birth at less than six months, he becomes refuted legally, meaning the Lawgiver refutes him in his saying 'I did not have intercourse with her' due to the existence of pregnancy at the time of divorce, and it has been reported in authentic reports: 'The child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone.'
