The answer is: the use of 'correct' here is based on exaggeration, because the failure in understanding the intended is in the strength of error according to them. 'That he divorced' - meaning: he divorced his wife saying he never had intercourse with her, and she had given birth before the divorce, so revocation is permissible for him because his statement that he never had intercourse with her, although it would require that the 'iddah is not valid. And revocation is valid in the two issues of pregnancy and childbirth despite his denial of intercourse, because the Law refutes him in his denial of intercourse, because the child belongs to the bed. 'And if he has seclusion [khalwah] with her and denies [intercourse], then no' - i.e., his revocation is not valid, because he denied intercourse, and there is no refutation of the Law for his denial, so his denial is evidence against him. [Commentary: 'And if he has seclusion' - past tense of khalwah, its summary: that he had seclusion with his wife then divorced her denying intercourse with her, his revocation is not valid, because the person is held to his confession as long as the Law or the testimony of existence does not refute him, and he confessed upon himself that he did not have intercourse with her, and revocation is only valid in the 'iddah of intercourse, not in the 'iddah of seclusion, unlike the previous issues of pregnancy and childbirth, because there the confession is invalidated by the existence of pregnancy and childbirth, but not here. If you say: the Law has made seclusion stand in the place of intercourse and as evidence for its existence, where it obligated 'iddah upon one who is divorced after seclusion without intercourse, and obligated the full dower after seclusion, so why did it not make the confession invalid here by the existence of seclusion and his confession of it? I say: seclusion does not necessitate intercourse, so his lying is not established legally, unlike childbirth and pregnancy, which are impossible without intercourse. As for the obligation of 'iddah in seclusion, it is for precaution, and the obligation of full dower, because she has handed over the subject of the contract, not because he had intercourse with her. 'Evidence against him' - where it nullifies the right of revocation, which is his right, and if he confessed to intercourse...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explaining the rulings on raj'ah (revocation of divorce) when the husband denies having had intercourse with his wife. The passage covers two scenarios: (1) the wife is pregnant or gives birth, and (2) the husband has had khalwah (seclusion) with his wife but denies intercourse. In the pregnancy/childbirth case, if the husband divorces his wife while denying intercourse, his denial is legally refuted by the birth of a child within six months of the divorce (or the existence of pregnancy), because the prophetic hadith 'the child belongs to the bed' establishes that a child born after six months from marriage is attributed to the husband. Thus his reasoning for denying raj'ah (that no 'iddah exists) is invalid, and raj'ah is valid. The commentator notes a criticism of the original author's wording, but defends it as a matter of rhetorical exaggeration. In the khalwah case, if the husband has had seclusion but denies actual intercourse, his denial is not refuted by the law, because khalwah does not necessarily imply intercourse; it is merely a legal presumption for 'iddah and mahr, but not for establishing his lying. Therefore, if he denies intercourse, his denial is accepted, and since he claims no 'iddah due to no intercourse, raj'ah is not valid. The commentator then answers an objection: why does the law treat khalwah as sufficient for 'iddah and full mahr but not to refute his denial? The answer is that those obligations are for precaution and for the wife's delivery of the marriage subject, not based on actual intercourse.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-raj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) within the 'iddah period, restoring the marriage without a new contract.
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed; metaphorically, the husband's right over the wife, which determines paternity of a child born within the marriage.
الخلوة (al-khalwah): Seclusion between a husband and wife in a private place where intercourse is possible, even if it did not actually occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband divorces his wife and denies having had intercourse with her. Later, the wife gives birth six months after the divorce. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, is the husband's revocation (raj'ah) valid?
A) Yes, because the child's birth at less than six months from the divorce proves the husband had intercourse, thus 'iddah exists.
B) No, because the husband's denial is accepted without evidence.
C) Yes, only if the husband admits to the child's paternity.
D) No, because revocation is not allowed after childbirth.

Key Takeaway
The birth of a child within six months of divorce refutes the husband's denial of intercourse, making raj'ah valid; mere seclusion (khalwah) does not have that effect.

Raj'a Valid Despite Denial with Childbirth
Explaining validity of raj'a in pregnancy/childbirth cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raj'a when the husband denies intercourse and the wife gives birth, and apply the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' to determine the validity of raj'a.

Core Idea
Childbirth proves intercourse: the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' overrides the husband's denial and validates raj'a.

This passage addresses a scenario where a husband denies having intercourse with his divorced wife, yet she gives birth within the waiting period. The Hanafi school rules that his denial is rejected because childbirth establishes lineage and thus intercourse. The proof is the prophetic hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the bed). You will learn the precise conditions and the exception for births within six months of marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled? Raj'a (revocation) when husband denies intercourse but wife gives birth. Ruling: sahih (valid).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash'. Quoted in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? It establishes the child's lineage to the husband, which implies that intercourse occurred. Therefore, the husband's denial is false.
4
Note the condition and exception
The ruling applies if the birth is more than six months after marriage. If less than six months, the child is not attributed to him, so his denial stands.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
This is consistent with the principle of firash, and it is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تصح الرجعة في مسألتي الحمل والولادة مع إنكاره الوطء ; لأن الشرع كذبه في إنكاره الوطء ; لأن الولد للفراش . الرجعة ; لكونها غير مدخول بها على زعمه، فلا عدة لها، فلا رجعة، لكن الشرع لما كذبه في إقراره جعل إقراره كأنه لم يكن ، وهذا إذا ولدت عنده لأكثر من ستة أشهر بعد النكاح، وأما لو ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت النكاح فلا ; لعدم ثبوت نسبه منه ، فلا يجعله الشارع كاذباً. قوله : لأن الولد للفراش ; هذا لفظ حديث أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما، وسيأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله في موضعه، ومعناه : إن الولد يكون لصاحب الفراش، ويثبت نسبه منه، فكل مولود ولد بعد النكاح لأكثر من ستة أشهر يكون ولداً له، وإن أنكر كونه ولداً له، ويلزم منه ثبوت أنه جامعها.
And indeed the revocation is valid in the two cases of pregnancy and childbirth with his denial of intercourse, because the Sharia has declared him a liar in his denial of intercourse because the child belongs to the bed. The revocation (is invalid because she is considered not consummated according to his claim, so she has no waiting period, so no revocation, but when the Sharia declared him a liar in his acknowledgment, it made his acknowledgment as if it never was). This is if she gives birth to him more than six months after marriage. But if she gives birth less than six months from the time of marriage, then no; because his lineage is not established from him, so the Sharia does not make him a liar. His statement: 'Because the child belongs to the bed'; this is the wording of a hadith narrated by Bukhari and Muslim and others. Its verification will come in its place, God willing. Its meaning: the child belongs to the owner of the bed, and his lineage is established from him. So every child born after marriage more than six months is considered his child, even if he denies it, and from that follows proof that he had intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage explains that a husband's revocation (raj'a) of a divorce is valid even if he denies having intercourse, provided there is evidence of pregnancy or childbirth. The proof is the hadith 'al-walad li al-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), which establishes the child's lineage to the husband in a valid marriage, thereby implying that intercourse occurred. The only exception is if the child is born less than six months after marriage, indicating conception before marriage; then the child is not attributed to him, and his denial stands. The hadith is cited from Bukhari and Muslim.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): Revocation of a revocable divorce within the waiting period.
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed, metaphorically referring to the husband to whom the child is attributed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Childbirth proves intercourse in a valid marriage; the husband's denial of intercourse cannot invalidate raj'a when the child is born more than six months after marriage.

intermediate
6 min
Raj'a Invalid with Seclusion and Husband's Denial
Ruling on raj'a after seclusion when husband denies intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raj'a after seclusion when the husband denies intercourse and explain why the denial is accepted.

Core Idea
Seclusion does not prove intercourse; the husband's denial stands, making raj'a invalid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a subtle point in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband secludes with his wife, then divorces her while denying intercourse, can he revoke the divorce? The answer is no. We will unpack the reasoning behind this ruling and how it differs from cases where pregnancy or childbirth provides clear evidence of consummation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with his wife, then pronounced divorce, and now denies that any intercourse occurred.
Seclusion here means being alone together in a place where intercourse is physically possible.
2
Apply the principle of iqrar
General rule: a person's acknowledgment (iqrar) is binding unless contradicted by Sharia or physical evidence. The husband acknowledges seclusion but denies intercourse.
The Sharia does not treat seclusion as definitive proof of intercourse.
3
Contrast with pregnancy/childbirth
In those cases, physical evidence (pregnancy, live birth) definitively proves intercourse, so the husband's denial is rejected. Here, seclusion alone does not necessitate intercourse.
Thus, his denial is accepted as truthful.
4
Analyze the objection and response
Objection: If seclusion triggers idda and full dower, why doesn't it prove intercourse? Response: Those obligations are based on caution (ihtiyat) and the wife's submission, not certainty.
Hence, the husband is not considered a liar.
5
Conclude the ruling
Raj'a is only valid if the wife is in idda from a consummated marriage. Here, she is in idda of seclusion only, so raj'a is invalid.
The husband must remarry with a new contract if desired.

قوله : وإن خلا ; ماض من الخلوة، حاصله : إنه خلا بامرأته ثم طلقها منكرا وطأها لا تصح رجعته ; لأن المراد يؤخذ بإقراره ما لم يكذبه الشرع أو شهادة الوجود، وقد أقر على نفسه بأنه لم يطأها، والرجعة إنما تصح في عدة المجامعة لا في عدة الخلوة ، بخلاف ما مر من مسألتي الحمل والولادة، فإن الإقرار هناك بطل بوجود الحمل والولادة، ولا كذلك هاهنا. فإن قلت : قد جعل الشرع الخلوة مقام الوطء ودليلا على وجوده، حيث أوجب العدة على من طلقت بعد الخلوة بدون الوطء، وأوجب المهر الكامل بعد الخلوة، فلم لم يجعل الإقرار باطلا هاهنا بوجود الخلوة، وإقراره بها. قلت : الخلوة لا تستلزم الوطء، فلا يثبت كذبه شرعا، بخلاف الولادة والحمل، فإنه لا يمكن بدون الوطء، وأما وجوب العدة في الخلوة فللاحتياط ووجوب كمال المهر ; لأنها سلمت المعقود عليه، لا لأنه وطئها. قوله : حجة عليه ; حيث يبطل حق الرجعة وهو حقه، ولو أقر بالوطء
His statement: 'And if he had seclusion (with her) and denies (intercourse), then no (revocation is valid).' His statement: 'And if he had seclusion' - past tense of seclusion. Its summary: He had seclusion with his wife, then he divorced her, denying that he had intercourse with her. His revocation is not valid, because the meant (principle) is that a person's acknowledgment is taken unless the Sharia or the testimony of existence refutes it. He acknowledged upon himself that he did not have intercourse with her. And revocation is only valid in the waiting period of one who had intercourse, not in the waiting period of seclusion. Contrary to what passed in the two cases of pregnancy and childbirth, because the acknowledgment there was nullified by the existence of pregnancy and childbirth. And it is not so here. If you say: The Sharia has made seclusion in the position of intercourse and as evidence for its existence, for it obligated the waiting period for one who was divorced after seclusion without intercourse, and obligated the full dower after seclusion. So why did it not make the acknowledgment invalid here with the existence of seclusion and his acknowledgment of it? I say: Seclusion does not necessitate intercourse, so he is not proven a liar by the Sharia. Unlike childbirth and pregnancy, because they cannot occur without intercourse. As for the obligation of the waiting period in seclusion, it is for caution, and the obligation of the full dower because she submitted the object of the contract (herself) to him, not because he had intercourse with her. His statement: 'It is a proof against him' i.e., it invalidates the right of revocation which is his right, even if he acknowledged the intercourse.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage explains that when a husband has valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with his wife, then divorces her while denying intercourse, his revocation (raj'a) is not valid. The reason is that his denial is accepted because seclusion does not necessarily prove intercourse. The general principle is that a person's acknowledgment (iqrar) is binding unless the Sharia or external evidence contradicts it. Here, the husband admits to seclusion but denies intercourse, and since seclusion does not imply intercourse, his denial stands. In contrast, pregnancy or childbirth is definitive proof of intercourse, so the husband's denial is rejected there. The text also addresses an objection: if seclusion triggers idda and full dower, why doesn't it make the husband a liar? The answer is that those obligations are based on caution (ihtiyat) and the wife's submission, not on certainty of intercourse. Thus, the husband's denial is a valid proof (hujja) against the validity of raj'a.

Key Terms
الخلوة (al-khalwa): Valid seclusion between husband and wife in private where nothing prevents intercourse.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): Revocation of divorce within the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the husband's denial is accepted since seclusion does not prove intercourse.

D) Because the divorce was not pronounced properly.

Key Takeaway
Raj'a is invalid when the husband denies intercourse after seclusion because seclusion does not prove consummation.

Birth Within Two Years Validates Earlier Raj'a
Ruling on raj'a when child born within two years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a prior raj'a is validated by a later birth and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A child born within two years of divorce proves consummation, validating the earlier raj'a.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi family law: a husband has seclusion with his wife, then divorces her, and later revokes the divorce. If the wife then gives birth within two years of the divorce, the earlier revocation is considered valid and the child's lineage is established to the husband. The birth within the maximum pregnancy period (two years) indicates that the wife was pregnant at the time of the divorce, proving that consummation occurred before separation. Without this evidence, such a revocation would be invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act in question is the husband's revocation (raj'a) of a divorce after seclusion. The ruling is that this raj'a is valid if the wife later gives birth within two years of the divorce.
The ruling hinges on the timing of the birth relative to the divorce.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The birth within two years proves that the wife was pregnant at the time of divorce, which means she must have been physically accessible (consummated) during the marriage. This contradicts the husband's denial of intercourse and confirms that the seclusion was effective.
Hanafi law sets the maximum pregnancy period at two years.
3
List the necessary conditions
1. Valid seclusion (khulwah sahihah) before divorce. 2. Divorce pronounced. 3. Revocation within 'iddah. 4. Birth of a child within two years from the time of divorce.
If any condition is missing, the raj'a may be invalid.
4
Identify exceptions and limits
If there was no seclusion, the birth does not validate the raj'a. Also, if the child is born more than two years after divorce, the lineage is not established and the raj'a is invalid. A birth less than six months from divorce is a separate case (premarital conception).
The two-year limit is specific to the Hanafi school.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Consider: Husband and wife have seclusion. He divorces her, then revokes. She gives birth 14 months later. The raj'a is valid because the birth is within two years. If she gave birth at 30 months, it would be invalid.
The key distinction is the two-year window.

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a husband had seclusion, divorced, then revoked, and later the wife gives birth to a child within two years from the divorce. The ruling is that the earlier revocation is valid and the child's lineage is established from him. The birth within the maximum pregnancy period (two years in Hanafi school) proves that the wife was pregnant at the time of divorce, implying that intercourse occurred before divorce, contradicting the husband's denial. Thus, the revocation is validated. The text also clarifies that if there was no seclusion, the revocation would not be valid because there is no evidence of prior intercourse. The sub-commentaries elaborate that the confirmation of dower by seclusion is not because seclusion equals intercourse, but because the wife made herself available. The condition of 'less than two years' is crucial; if the child is born after two years, the lineage is not established and the revocation is invalid. The maximum pregnancy period in Hanafi law is two years. If the child is born less than six months from marriage, it is a different case (premarital conception).

النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or parentage, established by birth in a valid marriage.

Validity of Revocation on Birth Within Two Years
Ruling on revocation with birth within two years of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a husband's revocation (raj'ah) is valid when the wife gives birth after divorce, and apply the Hanafi principle that birth within two years establishes consummation.

Core Idea
Birth within two years of divorce proves consummation, making revocation valid even if the husband denies intercourse.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi divorce law: a husband divorces his wife after having been in seclusion (khalwah) but denies any intercourse. He then revokes the divorce, and later the wife gives birth within two years. The commentary resolves the tension between the husband's denial and the birth by relying on the principle that the Lawgiver is cautious in establishing lineage. Understanding this ruling requires us to see how seclusion, the passage of time, and the birth of a child interact to determine the validity of revocation and the status of the wife as consummated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario involves: (1) valid seclusion (khalwah) between husband and wife; (2) husband denies intercourse; (3) husband issues a revocable divorce; (4) husband revokes the divorce during the waiting period; (5) wife gives birth within two years from the time of divorce.
The fact pattern assumes a valid marriage and seclusion without actual intercourse occurring.
2
Determine the ruling on revocation
The ruling is that the revocation (raj'ah) is valid (sahih). The birth of the child establishes that the wife was consummated (mudkhalun biha), even though the husband denies intercourse. The wife's denial of consummation is not accepted because it would nullify the husband's right to revoke and also affect the child's lineage.
Validity of revocation is contingent on the wife being in the waiting period; the birth confirms she was in it.
3
Analyze the proof: why does birth establish consummation?
The proof is based on istihsan (juridical preference) and the principle that the Shari'ah is cautious in establishing lineages. The birth within the maximum pregnancy duration (two years) is taken as evidence that intercourse must have occurred before the divorce, even if the husband denies it. This presumption avoids the greater harm of declaring the child illegitimate or of unknown parentage.
The proof is not from a direct text but from the overall objective of preserving lineage.
4
Understand the effect of seclusion (khalwah)
Khalwah (valid seclusion) is legally equivalent to actual intercourse for certain rulings, such as confirming the full mahr and making the wife a mudkhalun biha. However, the commentary clarifies that this is not because khalwah necessitates intercourse, but because the wife has surrendered (taslim) the benefits of her person to the husband, removing obstacles to possession.
Surrender (taslim) does not require actual taking possession (qabd); hence khalwah alone is sufficient for mahr and for considering the wife consummated in this context.
5
Apply the time limits (6 months to 2 years)
The birth must occur at least six months after the marriage (minimum pregnancy) and within two years (maximum pregnancy) from the time of divorce. If birth occurs before six months, it indicates pre-marital pregnancy (a separate issue). If after two years, revocation is invalid and lineage is not established because the maximum pregnancy duration has passed.
The two-year maximum is a Hanafi position based on narrations and juristic reasoning; other schools may hold a shorter maximum.

This indicates that seclusion stands in place of intercourse. The explanation of the repulsion: it is not because seclusion necessitates intercourse, or is in the ruling of intercourse in every respect, but because by seclusion she surrenders the benefits of the vulva to him and does not prevent him from obtaining them. [2] His statement: 'surrendered'; because surrender is the removal of obstacles between the one surrendering and the one to whom it is surrendered, and the one to whom it is surrendered is able to take possession, and that has occurred. Surrender does not necessitate taking possession, unlike the first action (intercourse); because pregnancy and the establishment of lineage necessitate taking possession, so denial would follow. Thus in al-Nihayah. [3] His statement: 'he divorces her'; meaning the woman with whom he secluded and whose intercourse he denied. [4] His statement: 'less than'; meaning from the time of divorce; he restricted it because if she gives birth after more than two years, the revocation is not valid, nor is the child's lineage established from him, because the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years. What is meant is that she gives birth between two years and six months; for if she gives birth less than six months, that is the previous issue. [0] His statement: 'is valid'; meaning it becomes evident after birth within less than two years that the earlier revocation was valid, because it becomes clear that she was consummated and that the revocation was within the waiting period. [6] His statement: 'then he divorced her'; this is mentioned by agreement, otherwise the ruling does not differ whether he denied intercourse first and then divorced her, or he divorced her first and then denied intercourse. [7] His statement: 'is established'; because the Lawgiver was cautious in establishing lineages, and made lineage established in every possible place as well, unless definitive evidence indicates its absence.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the chapter of revocation (raj'ah) in divorce. The ruling addresses a specific scenario: a husband divorces his wife after having been secluded with her (khulwah sahihah) and then denies having consummated the marriage. He then revokes the divorce within the waiting period. Subsequently, the wife gives birth to a child within two years from the time of divorce. The ruling states that the revocation is valid and the child's lineage is established from the husband. The commentary explains that this situation is connected to the issue of seclusion. It clarifies that the wife's denial of consummation is not accepted because it would invalidate the husband's right to revocation, and the apparent evidence (the birth) supports that consummation likely occurred. The text also distinguishes this from cases where there was no seclusion, in which case the wife's denial would be accepted. The commentary then delves into the reasoning behind the rule that the bride-price (mahr) is confirmed by seclusion alone, even without actual intercourse. This is because seclusion involves the wife surrendering the benefits of her vulva (manafi' al-bid') to the husband, and she does not prevent him from obtaining them. Surrender (taslim) is distinct from taking possession (qabd); the former is sufficient for the obligation of mahr. The later part of the commentary clarifies the time limits: if the birth occurs between six months (minimum pregnancy) and two years (maximum pregnancy) from the time of divorce, the revocation is valid and lineage is established. If the birth occurs before six months, it is a separate issue (likely referring to the case where the wife was already pregnant before marriage). If after two years, the revocation is invalid. The overarching principle is that the Lawgiver is cautious in establishing lineages, so lineage is presumed in every possible situation unless there is definitive proof otherwise.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-raj'ah): Revocation of a divorce; the husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.
الخلوة (al-khalwah): Seclusion of the husband and wife in a place where they are alone and can legally have intercourse, without any impediment.
النسب (an-nasab): Lineage or paternity; the legal attribution of a child to its father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Revocation is invalid, but lineage is established.

Key Takeaway
Birth within the maximum pregnancy duration from divorce establishes that the wife was consummated, thereby validating the husband's prior revocation and confirming the child's lineage.

Conditional Divorce Upon Birth and Subsequent Children
Ruling on conditional divorce and effects on subsequent multiple births

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce upon birth to cases of a subsequent birth within the maximum pregnancy period, and explain the underlying istihsan reasoning for establishing lineage and 'iddah.

Core Idea
To avoid zina, the husband is presumed to have had intercourse before the divorce if a child is born within the maximum pregnancy period.

This passage deals with a conditional divorce where a husband says, 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' After the first birth triggers the divorce, a second child is born. The commentator explains that if this second birth occurs within six months of the first, the child must have been conceived before the divorce. The husband is thus presumed to have had intercourse before the divorce – a presumption that establishes the child's lineage and validates any revocation. This ruling relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to protect against attributing zina to the Muslim couple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the legal status of the second child's lineage and the continuity of the 'iddah when a second birth occurs after a conditional divorce upon first birth.
The key is whether the second child is considered from before or after the divorce.
2
Understand the key variable
The interval between the first and second birth is crucial: if less than six months, the second child must have existed in the womb before the first birth; if more, it could be a new pregnancy.
Minimum pregnancy in Hanafi is six months; maximum is two years.
3
Locate the proof (istihsan)
The ruling is based on istihsan: to avoid attributing zina to the husband or wife, we presume the husband had intercourse before the divorce, making the second child legitimate.
The dalil is not Quran/Hadith but a legal presumption rooted in protecting lineage and avoiding sin.
4
Trace the reasoning
If we did not presume intercourse before divorce, the second child would be from after the divorce (when intercourse is forbidden), leading to a charge of zina. Since the wife did not declare her 'iddah ended, the safer assumption is that the child belongs to the husband.
The husband's denial of intercourse is deemed false to preserve the child's lineage.
5
Apply the rule to different intervals
For intervals ≤6 months: child is pre-divorce; for >6 months but ≤2 years: still presumed pre-divorce if wife did not confirm 'iddah ended; for >2 years: lineage is not established.
This is the Hanafi position; other schools may have different maximum periods.

Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses a conditional divorce scenario: a husband says to his wife, 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' She gives birth, thereby triggering the divorce. Subsequently, she gives birth to another child. The commentary examines how this affects the waiting period and the lineage of the second child. The key point is that the wife does not admit that her waiting period (from the first birth) has ended; the child from the second birth may still be in the womb. Since the minimum pregnancy is six months and the maximum is two years, it is possible that the second child was conceived before the divorce (i.e., the first birth). To avoid attributing the child to a forbidden relationship (zina) that would result from intercourse after the divorce, the husband is presumed to have had intercourse before the divorce (i.e., the first birth). This presumption makes the revocation (if he had divorced and then revoked) valid. The commentary explains that if the wife had admitted that her waiting period ended, then later gave birth within two years of the divorce, the child's lineage would not be established from the husband. The discussion also clarifies the phrase 'with two pregnancies' (bi-batnayn) – meaning separate pregnancies, not twins. If the interval between births is six months or less, it indicates the second child existed in the womb before the first birth, so it does not imply new intercourse after the first birth. This reasoning relies on the principle of caution in lineage and avoiding accusations of zina.

Key Terms
الطلاق المعلق (at-talaq al-mu'allaq): A conditional divorce where the divorce is suspended upon the occurrence of a future event, such as giving birth.
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
ببطنين (bi-batnayn): Literally 'with two bellies', meaning separate pregnancies (not twins).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
If a second child is born within the maximum pregnancy period (two years) after a conditional divorce upon first birth, and the wife has not acknowledged the end of her 'iddah, the child's lineage is established from the husband based on istihsan to avoid zina.

Revocation Established by Second Birth After Conditional Divorce
Explaining when a second birth constitutes revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a second birth after a conditional divorce constitutes a valid revocation and explain the evidence based on the minimum pregnancy period.

Core Idea
A second birth more than six months after the first proves post-divorce intercourse, establishing valid revocation.

This passage addresses a specific question in family law: when a husband says "If you give birth, you are divorced," and the wife gives birth, then later gives birth again, does the second birth revoke the divorce? The Hanafi jurists answer using the established medical minimum of pregnancy (six months) and the presumption that a Muslim avoids adultery. Understanding this ruling helps you see how evidence and legal presumptions interact to protect legitimacy and marital rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
The husband suspends divorce on the condition of birth. After the first birth, the divorce takes effect. Then a second birth occurs. Does this second birth indicate that the husband revoked the divorce by having intercourse during the waiting period?
The waiting period is still ongoing if the wife has not admitted its expiry.
2
Understand the presumption of lawful intercourse
The jurists presume that the husband's actions are lawful. If the second child's conception could only have occurred after the divorce, then the husband must have had intercourse during the waiting period, which is a valid revocation. This avoids attributing adultery.
It is easier to accuse the husband of lying about denying intercourse than to accuse him of adultery.
3
Apply the minimum pregnancy duration as evidence
The minimum pregnancy is six months (a known fact). If the second birth occurs more than six months after the first, then conception must have occurred after the first birth (and after the divorce). Thus, the second birth proves post-divorce intercourse.
If the interval is less than six months, the child was conceived before the first birth, so no post-divorce intercourse.
4
Distinguish the time intervals
Interval ≤ 6 months: child was conceived before the first birth → no post-divorce intercourse → no revocation. Interval > 6 months: child conceived after the first birth → post-divorce intercourse → revocation.
The maximum pregnancy is two years, but as long as she does not admit the end of the waiting period, the possibility remains.
5
Recognize that the child is evidence, not the revocation itself
The second child is not literally the revocation; rather, its birth is evidence that a revocation occurred through intercourse. The revocation is the act of intercourse, not the child.
This resolves a common misunderstanding.

Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific ruling on revocation (رجعة) in the context of a conditional divorce. The case: a husband says to his wife, 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' She gives birth, and then later gives birth to another child. The key is to determine whether the second birth indicates a valid revocation. The jurists reason based on the minimum pregnancy of six months. If the second birth occurs more than six months after the first, then the second child must have been conceived after the first birth and after the divorce. Since the husband had intercourse during the waiting period, that intercourse constitutes a revocation of the divorce. If the second birth occurs within six months, then the child was conceived before the first birth, so there was no post-divorce intercourse, and no revocation. The text emphasizes that we presume the husband acted lawfully and that the child is legitimate, rather than assuming adultery, because it is easier to accuse him of lying about intercourse than to accuse him of adultery. The passage also clarifies that the child itself is not the revocation, but rather evidence of the revocation. Finally, it mentions the case of three births leading to three divorces, with the second and third children serving as evidence of revocation, and that the woman must observe her waiting period by menstruation.

Key Terms
رجعة (rajʿa): Revocation of a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) by the husband during the waiting period, re-establishing the marital relationship without a new contract.
بطن (baṭn): A pregnancy or birth; used to denote a single gestation. 'Two battins' means two separate pregnancies or births.
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage. For a divorced woman not pregnant, it is three menstrual cycles.
علوق (ʿulūq): Conception; the time when pregnancy begins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The second birth does not affect the divorce; the wife remains divorced.
B) The second birth proves that the husband revoked the divorce after the first birth.
C) The second child is illegitimate because the husband denied intercourse.
D) The second birth is irrelevant because the waiting period ended after the first birth.

Key Takeaway
A second birth more than six months after a conditional divorce establishes revocation of the divorce, based on the minimum pregnancy period and the presumption that a Muslim avoids adultery.

The Second Birth as Evidence of Raj'ah
Explains that a second birth after six months indicates raj'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a second birth during the 'iddah serves as evidence of raj'ah and explain the rationale based on the time gap between births.

Core Idea
A second birth six months after the first proves a new intercourse, which is a revocable return (raj'ah).

This lesson examines a specific rule from the Hanafi school: how the timing of a divorced woman's second birth can serve as legal evidence of a revocable return (raj'ah) during the 'iddah period. The commentary clarifies when two births are considered separate pregnancies and how this indicates that the husband resumed marital relations, thereby effecting a valid return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Condition
Recognize that the passage states that a second birth after six months is evidence of raj'ah. The key condition is the time gap between the two births.
2
Understand 'Two Wombs' (Batnayn)
The term 'two wombs' means two separate pregnancies separated by at least six months. If less, they are considered one pregnancy.
In Hanafi fiqh, multiple births from the same pregnancy count as one batn.
3
Grasp the Rationale
The second birth indicates a new intercourse after the divorce. Since intercourse during the 'iddah is itself a raj'ah, the second birth becomes evidence of that return.
The child is not the raj'ah but proof of it.
4
Note the Time Criterion
The gap must be six months or more. Even more than ten years is acceptable as long as the woman does not acknowledge the end of the 'iddah.
Menopause is the only limit to the 'iddah's extension.
5
Recognize the Evidential Nature
The passage explicitly states that the second child itself is not the raj'ah; it is evidence of the raj'ah.
This avoids confusion between the legal act and its proof.

Source Text

وأما إذا كان أكثر من ستة أشهر يجعل علوقُ الولد الثاني بوطء حادث بعـد الطلاق، والوطء في زمان العدة رجعة.

[١] قوله : فهو رجعة ؛ أي الولد الثاني رجعة، ونسبت الرجعة إليه ؛ لكونه دليلاً على وطء حادث بعد الطلاق وهو رجعة. (۱) ولو لأكثر من عشر سنين ما لم تقرّ بانقضاء العدّة ؛ لأن امتداد الطهر لا غاية له إلا اليأس. ينظر: «الدر المختار (۲ : ٥٣٥).
So it is raj'ah. The intended meaning of 'two wombs' is that between the first birth and the second there are six months or more. If less, it is one womb. Raj'ah is established only because she was divorced by the first birth; the second birth indicates that he returned to her after the first birth, so that intercourse becomes lawful. If the two births are of one womb, raj'ah is not established because the conception of the second child was before the first birth. [1] He said: 'So it is raj'ah', i.e., the second child is raj'ah. Raj'ah is attributed to it because it is evidence of a new intercourse after divorce, which is raj'ah. [2] He said: 'The intended meaning...' etc., refuting anyone who might think that 'two wombs' means two times; the gist is that multiplicity or unity of the womb is according to the custom of jurists. [3] He said: 'Or more', even more than ten years, for example, as long as she does not acknowledge the end of the waiting period, because the duration of purity has no limit except menopause. [4] He said: 'Raj'ah is only established' indicates that his saying 'so it is raj'ah' does not mean that the second child itself is raj'ah, but rather that it is evidence of raj'ah, and that raj'ah is established by it. [0] He said: 'So that intercourse becomes lawful' – indeed, intercourse with a divorced woman without intending raj'ah is unlawful, and that is far from the character of a Muslim. (1) And even if more than ten years, as long as she does not acknowledge the end of the waiting period, because the duration of purity has no limit except menopause. See al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:535).

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'فهو رجعة' (so it is raj'ah). The commentator first clarifies the meaning of 'two wombs' (بطنين): it refers to two separate pregnancies with a gap of six months or more. If less, it counts as one pregnancy. The ruling is that if a divorced woman gives birth twice, the second birth indicates a new sexual intercourse occurring during the 'iddah, which constitutes a revocable return (raj'ah). This is because without such a return, intercourse would be unlawful. The numbered notes then elaborate: the second child itself is not the raj'ah but evidence of it; the duration of purity can extend indefinitely until menopause; and the purpose is to make intercourse lawful, as it would otherwise be sinful. The footnote confirms that the gap can be more than ten years as long as the 'iddah is not acknowledged to have ended.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The act of a husband taking back his wife during her 'iddah after a revocable divorce.
بطن (batn): A pregnancy or a delivery; here, a separate pregnancy (womb) with a gap of six months or more.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a second birth six months after the first during the 'iddah period is legally considered evidence that the husband has made a revocable return (raj'ah) through intercourse.

Conditional Divorce with 'Kullama' and Separate Births
Basic rule for three separate pregnancies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a conditional divorce using 'kullama' when the wife gives birth three times from distinct pregnancies, and explain how the second birth functions as a return (raj'ah).

Core Idea
With 'kullama', each birth from a separate pregnancy triggers a divorce; the second birth simultaneously acts as a return.

This lesson unpacks the core scenario of a conditional divorce where a husband says 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced.' If the wife delivers three children from three different pregnancies, the first birth divorces her, the second birth is considered a return to the marriage (raj'ah) and then a second divorce, and the third birth effects the final divorce. Understanding this requires careful attention to the implications of the word 'kullama' and the concept of 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its implication
The man uses 'kullama' (whenever), which implies repetition. Each occurrence of the condition (a birth) potentially triggers a divorce. Confirm that the condition is a talil (cause) for the divorce.
Hanafi usul: kullama indicates repetition; each event is independent.
2
Apply the first birth – first divorce
When the first child is born, the first divorce occurs. The wife begins her 'iddah (waiting period) of three menstrual cycles.
3
Analyze the second birth – return and second divorce
The second birth must occur from a separate pregnancy after the first divorce. Conception during the 'iddah is attributed to a new intercourse, which constitutes a return (raj'ah) to marriage. Immediately, the second divorce falls because the condition (birth) is met again.
The second birth proves that the husband resumed marital relations during the 'iddah, which counts as a return.
4
Apply the third birth – third divorce
The third birth, again from a separate pregnancy, triggers the third divorce. After this, the marriage is irrevocably terminated (talaq ba'in).
The wife must now observe a fresh 'iddah for the third divorce.
5
Note the 'iddah calculation
For the third divorce, the 'iddah is by three menstrual cycles (if she menstruates) or three months (if she is menopausal). This 'iddah is independent from the previous one.

[٦]قوله : كالثالث؛ لأنها إذا جاءت بالولدِ الأوّل وقع الطلاق، وصارت معتدة، وبالثانية صار راجعاً، فإنّه يجعل العلوقُ بوطء حادث في العدة، ويقع الطلاق الثاني

(۱) ولو لأكثر من عشر سنين ما لم تقرّ بانقضاء العدّة ؛ لأن امتداد الطهر لا غاية له إلا اليأس. ينظر: «الدر المختار (۲ : ٥٣٥).

(And in 'whenever she gives birth, then she gives birth to three wombs, the three [divorces] occur, and the second child is a return like the third, and upon her is the waiting period by menstruation.) The first birth, so that it is a return. [6] He said: 'Like the third' – because when she brings the first child, the divorce occurs, and she becomes a woman in 'iddah; with the second, he becomes a returner, for the conception is attributed to a new intercourse during the 'iddah, and the second divorce occurs. Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for a conditional divorce using the word 'kullama' (whenever). If a man says 'Whenever you give birth, you are divorced', and the wife gives birth to three separate children (each from a distinct pregnancy), the first birth triggers the first divorce. The second birth is considered a return (raj'ah) because it proves intercourse during the 'iddah, and then the second divorce falls. The third birth similarly triggers the third divorce. The 'iddah for the third divorce is by three menstrual cycles unless she is menopausal. The text then examines special cases: if all three births occur in the same pregnancy, only two divorces fall (by the first two births) because the 'iddah ends with the second birth, making the third birth coincident with the end of 'iddah, so no divorce. If the first two are in one pregnancy and the third in another, only one divorce falls by the first, the 'iddah ends with the second, and no divorce by the third. If the first is separate and the second and third are in one pregnancy, two divorces fall by the first and second, and the 'iddah ends with the third, so no divorce.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A conditional word meaning 'whenever' indicating repetition; here, each occurrence of the condition triggers the divorce.
بطن (batn): A pregnancy or a delivery; here, 'different batn' means separate pregnancies.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry; here, for a revocable divorce, it ends with three menstrual cycles or three months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

For conditional divorce with 'kullama', each separate birth triggers a divorce, and the second birth additionally constitutes a return.

Special Cases: Same Pregnancy and Mixed Births
Special cases: births in same pregnancy or mixed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces when births occur in the same pregnancy or in mixed combinations, applying the principle that the 'iddah termination prevents divorce.

Core Idea
When multiple births share a pregnancy, only one divorce occurs; subsequent births during that same 'iddah do not cause divorce.

The previous lesson covered the standard case of separate pregnancies. However, if the wife gives birth to multiple children from one pregnancy (e.g., twins), the ruling changes. This lesson covers the three possible mixed scenarios: all in one pregnancy, first two together and third separate, first separate and last two together. Understanding these nuances is essential for accurate fatwa on conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Case 1: All three births in one pregnancy
If a single pregnancy yields three children (e.g., triplets), the first birth triggers the first divorce. The second birth does not trigger a second divorce because it is part of the same batn. The third divorce would normally occur by the third birth, but the passage says two divorces fall by the first two births, not by the third. Reason: The 'iddah from the first divorce ends with the second birth? Actually the text: 'ولو كانوا ببطن تقع ثنتان بالأولين لا بالثالث' – two divorces by the first two, not by the third. So only two divorces total? But the first birth is one divorce, second birth is second divorce? That would be two divorces. Then why does the third not cause a divorce? Because the 'iddah ends with the second birth? But the 'iddah should be three cycles, not by birth. The reasoning is more subtle: The condition 'whenever you give birth' applies to each birth event. But when multiple births occur in one pregnancy, they are considered one event? Actually the passage states: because the 'iddah ends with the second birth? It says 'لانقضاء العدة به' – because the 'iddah ends with it (the second birth). How does the second birth end the 'iddah? The explanation is missing in the text. But we can follow the text's ruling.
The text does not fully explain the logic, but the ruling is clear: only two divorces occur in this scenario.
2
Case 2: First two in one pregnancy, third separate
The first birth triggers the first divorce. The second birth (from the same pregnancy) does not trigger any divorce because it is part of the same event. The third birth, being from a separate pregnancy, triggers a second divorce? Wait, the passage says: 'ولو كان الأولان في بطن والثالث في بطن تقع واحدة بالأول، وتنقضي العدة بالثاني، ولا يقع شيء بالثالث' – one divorce by the first, the 'iddah ends with the second, and nothing by the third. So only one divorce total! Because the second birth ends the 'iddah, so the third birth is no longer within the 'iddah period? But the condition still occurs, but it is after the 'iddah has ended, so the divorce does not fall? This needs clarification.
The principle: if the condition occurs after the 'iddah has ended, it does not trigger divorce because the marriage has already been dissolved? Actually after the first divorce, the marriage is revocable. If the 'iddah ends without return, the marriage ends. In this case, the second birth ends the 'iddah (how?), and thus the third birth is after the final separation, so no divorce.
3
Case 3: First separate, second and third in one pregnancy
The first birth triggers the first divorce. The second and third births are from one pregnancy. The passage: 'ولو كان الأول في بطن، والثاني والثالث في بطن، تقع ثنتان بالأول والثاني، وتنقضي العدة بالثالث، فلا يقع به شيء' – two divorces fall by the first and second, the 'iddah ends with the third, so nothing by the third. So two divorces total: first divorce by first birth, second divorce by second birth, third birth does not cause divorce because the 'iddah ends with it? Actually the 'iddah ends with the third birth, so the third birth coincides with the end of 'iddah and thus no divorce.
This is consistent with the pattern: the last birth in a pregnancy ends the 'iddah and prevents further divorce.

Adornment and Travel for the Revocably Divorced Woman
Rulings on adornment and travel for a revocably divorced woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the revocably divorced woman, the irrevocably divorced woman, and the widow regarding adornment and travel, and state the Hanafi rulings for each.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman may adorn herself to encourage return but may not travel; the marriage is partially intact.

In Hanafi fiqh, the 'iddah (waiting period) after a revocable divorce (raj'ah) preserves the marriage bond to a degree—inheritance, intercourse, and even the possibility of return remain. This lesson examines two practical consequences: the permissibility of the wife adorning herself, and the prohibition of the husband traveling with her during this period. The text contrasts her situation with that of the irrevocably divorced woman and the widow, who face stricter restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states: (1) the revocably divorced woman may adorn herself (it is recommended), and (2) she should not be taken on a journey. Note that 'may adorn herself' is a permission/recommendation, while 'should not be taken' is a prohibition directed at the husband.
The prohibition on travel implies it is haram for the husband to take her without necessity.
2
Understand the reasoning
The reason for permitting adornment is so that the husband may desire to take her back (raj'ah), which is itself recommended. The prohibition on travel protects the integrity of the 'iddah and the possibility of return.
The marriage is partially intact—inheritance and sexual intimacy remain permissible.
3
Distinguish from the irrevocably divorced woman
The text explicitly restricts these rulings to the revocably divorced woman by using the word 'al-raj'iyy'. An irrevocably divorced woman (ba'in) must not adorn herself during 'iddah because looking at her is prohibited and return is impossible without a new marriage contract.
The irrevocable divorce severs the marriage completely.
4
Distinguish from the widow
A widow in 'iddah (from death of husband) must observe ihdad—refraining from adornment, perfume, and beautiful clothes—by explicit text (Quran and hadith). This is a wajib (obligatory) restriction, unlike the revocably divorced woman's situation.
Ihdad is not required for the divorced woman, only for the widow.
5
Note the source and authority
The commentary cites 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' and refers to 'al-Hidayah' and 'Fath al-Qadir'. These are authoritative Hanafi sources. The ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah (the well-known transmitted position of the school).
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is that adornment is permissible and recommended for the revocably divorced woman.

Commentary
This passage addresses the etiquette of a woman in revocable divorce ('iddah of raj'ah). It states two rulings: first, she may adorn herself (and it is recommended) so that her husband may desire to take her back. Second, he should not travel with her. The commentary explains that this is because the marriage bond is still partially intact—inheritance between them continues, and intercourse and its preliminaries are permissible (provided the intention of return exists). إحداد (ihdad): Mourning by refraining from adornment and perfume, required for a widow.

Witnesses for Reconciliation and Intercourse as Raj'a: Hanafi View
Inter-madhhab dispute on witnessing and intercourse as raj'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses for raj'a and explain why intercourse constitutes valid raj'a, distinguishing the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a revocably divorced woman is itself a valid reconciliation; witnesses are recommended, not required.

In this passage, the author addresses two connected issues: the witnessing of reconciliation and the legal effect of intercourse during the revocable divorce period. The Hanafi school holds that sexual intimacy is a stronger sign of taking back the wife than mere words, while Imam al-Shafi'i insists on a verbal declaration. We will explore the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on witnesses and intercourse
Identify what the text says about witnesses (ishhad) and whether intercourse counts as raj'a.
Witnesses are mustahabb (recommended), not wajib. Intercourse itself suffices as raj'a.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Contrast the Hanafi view with al-Shafi'i's requirement: verbal declaration, not action.
The text says 'عندنا' vs 'عند الشافعي'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand the rationale: the marriage bond persists during revocable divorce, so intercourse is a marital right that implies reconciliation.
No direct hadith cited; reasoning based on continuity of contract.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: automatic reconciliation vs need for verbal statement.
Affects inheritance, remarriage, and ongoing rights.
5
Apply the Quranic verse to travel prohibition
The verse 'Do not expel them' is used to prohibit traveling with her before raj'a.
Travel would imply leaving the marital home, which is forbidden during idda.

The chapter of divorce: the section on reconciliation. Until he brings witnesses to his taking her back. And he may have intercourse with her. Until he brings witnesses to his taking her back. And he may have intercourse with her: This is according to us (Hanafis), but according to al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with the revocably divorced woman until he takes her back verbally, while with us, intercourse itself constitutes taking back. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the revocably divorced woman. The main text states that the husband should not travel with her without witnesses to reconciliation, but he may have intercourse with her. The commentary clarifies that bringing witnesses is recommended (mustahabb) to avoid suspicion, not obligatory. The key dispute is highlighted: according to the Hanafi school, intercourse with a revocably divorced woman during her idda constitutes a valid reconciliation (raj'a) without the need for a verbal statement. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that intercourse is not permissible until the husband verbally declares raj'a. The Hanafi position is based on the idea that the marriage bond continues in some respects, and intercourse is one of the rights of marriage, so it implies the intention to take back. The passage also reiterates the prohibition of traveling with her before reconciliation, citing the Quranic verse 'Do not expel them from their houses' (Surah al-Talaq 65:1), which implies she must remain in the marital home. The source referenced is al-Binaya.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of producing witnesses; here, having witnesses present when reconciling with a revocably divorced wife.
رجعة (raj'a): Taking back a revocably divorced wife during idda; can be done by intercourse according to Hanafis.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse; in context, it is considered a valid means of raj'a in Hanafi law.

Witnessing and Intercourse in Revocable Divorce
Explains ruling on witnessing and intercourse as return in revocable divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that intercourse with a revocably divorced wife constitutes a valid return (raj'ah) and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Intercourse itself is a valid return; witnessing is only recommended.

This passage from the chapter on revocable divorce (raj'ah) addresses two key questions: Is it necessary to have witnesses when returning to one's divorced wife? And does intercourse alone suffice as a return? The Hanafi school takes a pragmatic stance, viewing intercourse as a clear indicator of the husband's intention to retain his wife, while the Shafi'i school insists on a verbal declaration. Let's examine the evidence and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on witnessing
Identify that the author says 'until he has witnesses' and clarify from the commentary that this is recommended (mustahab) to avoid accusation, not obligatory.
The command is permissive, not imperative.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling on intercourse as return
Note the text states 'he may have intercourse with her', indicating that intercourse itself is a valid act of return (raj'ah) without a separate verbal declaration.
This is the core of the dispute.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
Examine the verse 'Do not evict them from their houses' (Surah al-Talaq 65:1). The Hanafis derive that the wife remains under the husband's authority, implying he can have intercourse, which in turn implies return.
The dalala is zanni but supported by practice.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school requires a verbal declaration for a valid return; intercourse alone is ambiguous. Understand their reasoning and the Hanafi response.
The dispute is over the interpretation of the same text.
5
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In real life, if a Hanafi husband has intercourse without words, the marriage is reinstated. Under Shafi'i, it is not until he says 'I return to you'.
This difference affects the legal status of subsequent actions.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school regarding the validity of intercourse as a form of revocable return (raj'ah). The text begins by stating that a husband should have witnesses for his return to his revocably divorced wife, and that he may have intercourse with her. The commentary clarifies that the command to have witnesses is on the basis of recommendation (istihbab) to avoid accusations, not obligation. The principle ruling is that intercourse itself constitutes a return (raj'ah) in the Hanafi school, whereas Imam al-Shafi'i holds that intercourse does not suffice; a verbal declaration is required. The Hanafi proof is derived from the Quranic verse 'Do not evict them from their houses' (Surah al-Talaq, 65:1), which indicates that the divorced woman remains under the husband's authority during the iddah, implying that intercourse is permissible and functions as a return. Furthermore, traveling with the wife before formal return is prohibited, and if the husband travels with her while silent, it is considered a return by implication (dalalah). The commentary also references al-Binayah and other Hanafi sources to affirm this position.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The return of a husband to his revocably divorced wife during her iddah, reinstating the marriage without a new contract.
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of producing witnesses to testify that one has returned to his divorced wife.
رجعة دلالة (raj'ah dalalah): Return by implication, such as when the husband travels with his wife silently, which indicates his intention to retain her.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intercourse with a revocably divorced wife during iddah is a valid return (raj'ah) without need for witnesses or verbal declaration; witnessing is only recommended.

Conditions for a Divorced Woman to Become Halal
Explains when a divorced woman becomes permissible to her ex-husband after divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions required for a woman divorced thrice to become halal for her first husband and apply the rule to the case of a slave woman.

Core Idea
After three divorces, a free woman requires marriage, consummation, and iddah with another man to become halal.

This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah clarifies a key ruling in divorce law. The Quranic verse (2:230) makes it clear that after three divorces, the woman is not lawful for the first husband until she marries another man. However, the Hanafi school unpacks what 'marriage' entails: a valid contract, actual consummation, and completion of the iddah. The text also addresses the case of a slave woman and the exception for the first husband during iddah for ba'in divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the subject: a divorced woman becoming halal. The ruling: after three divorces (free) or two (slave), she becomes halal only after three conditions.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (2:230) is cited. Identify it as the proof.
The verse explicitly states the condition for halal after three divorces.
3
Analyse the conditions (arkan and wajibat)
The three conditions: valid marriage (rukn), consummation (rukn), iddah (wajib). Also note the exception for the first husband in ba'in divorce.
Consummation includes actual penetration; ejaculation is not required.
4
Understand the consensus-based exception
The objection from verse 2:235 and the response: first husband is exempted by consensus because no lineage confusion.
Otherwise, marriage during iddah is prohibited for others.
5
Apply the rule to a slave woman
Two divorces for a slave woman equal three for a free woman, by analogy and because divorce and iddah follow the woman's status.

[0] قوله : حتى يطأها ؛ أشار بإطلاقه إلى أنه أعم من أن يكون حقيقة، ومن أن يكون حكماً ، كما إذا تزوجت بمجبوب فحبلت منه ، وإلى أنه لا يشترط الإنزال ، بل مجرد الإيلاج ؛ لأنَّ الإنزال كمالُ الوطء ، والنص الذي يدلّ على اشتراط الوطء لا يدل عليه.

A chapter on what makes a divorced woman permissible. And the marriage of a woman who is irrevocably divorced (ba'in) with less than three divorces in her iddah and after it. And a free woman does not become permissible after three, nor a slave woman after two, until another man has intercourse with her in a valid marriage, and the iddah of that divorce (from the second husband) passes, whether by divorce or death. [1] His saying: 'and marriage' — it is either connected to his saying 'his intercourse', in which case his saying 'in her iddah' is related to the marriage, meaning the husband may marry a ba'in woman (passive participle: one who was divorced irrevocably once or twice), or it is a subject with the predicate 'in her iddah' meaning it is permissible or established in her iddah and after the iddah. [2] His saying: 'in her iddah' — an objection is raised that his saying (Quran): 'And do not resolve on the marriage contract until the prescribed period has reached its end' (2:235) indicates the prohibition of marriage during the iddah absolutely, whether the marrying partner is the first husband or another. And it is answered that the husband is excepted by consensus; others are prevented from it to avoid confusion of lineage of children, and there is no confusion in the marriage of the first husband, for it only occurs when there is mixture of waters. [3] His saying: 'and after it' — meaning without need for a [third] husband's permission; indeed permissibility is not completely negated without three, as indicated by His saying: 'And if he divorces her [three times], she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' (2:230), where the removal of permissibility is attached to the third divorce. [4] His saying: 'nor a slave woman after two' — because as mentioned, two divorces for a slave woman are like three for a free woman, whether her husband is a slave or free; for divorce and iddah in our school are both based on the woman. [5] His saying: 'until he has intercourse with her' — it indicates by its generality that it includes actual intercourse and constructive intercourse, such as if she marries an impotent man and becomes pregnant from him, and that ejaculation is not required, but mere penetration; because ejaculation is the perfection of intercourse, and the text that indicates the requirement of intercourse does not indicate it.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that explains the conditions under which a divorced woman becomes permissible (halal) to her first husband. The text begins by stating that a man may marry his wife who is in a state of ba'in (irrevocable) divorce with less than three divorces, whether during her iddah or after it, without needing a third party. However, for a free woman after three divorces, and a slave woman after two, she does not become permissible until she marries another man, consummates the marriage (intercourse), and completes the iddah from that marriage (either by divorce or death). The commentary addresses objections: the verse in Surah al-Baqarah (2:235) prohibits marriage during iddah, but by consensus the first husband is exempted because confusion of lineage is not possible. For a slave woman, two divorces are equivalent to three for a free woman due to the rule that divorce and iddah follow the woman's status. Finally, the requirement of intercourse is explained: it must be actual penetration, not necessarily ejaculation, and cases of constructive intercourse (e.g., pregnancy from an impotent husband) also suffice. The proof is from the Quranic verse (2:230), which explicitly states the condition for the woman to become halal after three divorces.

Key Terms
مبانة (mubānah): A woman who has been given an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) with one or two divorces, not three.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse, which in this context means actual penetration, not necessarily ejaculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for a woman who has been divorced three times to become halal for her first husband?
A) She must marry another man and have a verbal divorce.
B) She must marry another man, consummate the marriage, and complete the iddah.
C) She must only remarry the first husband with a new contract.
D) She must wait for her first husband to perform tahlil.

Key Takeaway
A free woman after three divorces (or a slave after two) requires a valid marriage, consummation, and completion of the iddah with another man before she becomes halal for the first husband.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for a Thrice-Divorced Woman to Be Halal
Expands the basic ruling with conditions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and state the three conditions under which a thrice-divorced free woman becomes permissible for her first husband according to the Hanafi school, and explain the linguistic and legal basis for requiring consummation.

Core Idea
A thrice-divorced woman is halal only after valid marriage, consummation, and completed iddah with another man.

The issue of a thrice-divorced woman (al-muṭallaqah thalāthan) returning to her first husband is a sensitive and often misunderstood area of Hanafi fiqh. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqāyah lays out the precise conditions—marriage, consummation, and iddah—and defends them against a minority opinion. Understanding these conditions is essential not only for knowing the ruling but also for appreciating how the Hanafi school interprets sacred texts to derive practical law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its three conditions
Read the opening sentence of the source: 'until someone else has intercourse with her in a valid marriage, and [until] the iddah of his divorce or his death passes.' This establishes three sequential conditions: (1) a valid marriage contract (nikāḥ ṣaḥīḥ) with a second husband, (2) actual intercourse (waṭʾ) with him, and (3) completion of the iddah from that marriage (either by divorce or death).
For a slave woman, two divorces have the same effect as three for a free woman.
2
Understand the validity and scope of the second marriage
The second marriage must be valid (ṣaḥīḥ)—fornication (zinā) or a defective marriage (nikāḥ fāsid) does not suffice. The second husband can be any man other than the first, including a minor (murahīq), eunuch (khaṣīy), impotent (ʿanīn), one with amputated penis (majbūb), or a dhimmī for a dhimmī woman.
The commentary cites 'Al-Bahar' for the inclusive list.
3
Analyze the definition of intercourse (waṭʾ) in this context
The author states that waṭʾ includes both actual penetration (ḥaqīqī) and constructive intercourse (ḥukmī), e.g., pregnancy from a man with a severed penis. Ejaculation is not required—mere insertion (al-ilāj) suffices. This broader definition is based on the linguistic scope of the term and the absence of any text specifying ejaculation.
The hadith 'until she tastes the sweetness and bitterness' also supports this, but is not quoted in the passage.
4
Examine the primary proof from Quran 2:230 and its interpretation
The majority (including Hanafis) require intercourse because they interpret 'nikāḥ' in the verse 'ḥattā tankiḥa zawjan ghayrahu' as 'intercourse.' They argue that 'nikāḥ' linguistically means 'union' and is used for intercourse in Quran 4:6. Even if it means 'contract', the requirement of intercourse is established by other evidence (sumah). The minority view of Saʿīd ibn al-Musayyab, who takes the literal meaning as 'marriage contract,' is thus rejected.
This is a classic case of linguistic usul: determining whether a word carries its haqīqah (literal) or majāz (figurative) meaning.
5
Appreciate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute has a tangible effect: if only a marriage contract is made without intercourse, under the Hanafi view the woman remains prohibited to the first husband; under the minority view she would become permissible. The Hanafi position requires actual consummation, which is the muftā bihī (operative ruling) today.
The tarjīḥ (preference) is based on the strength of the majority evidence and the prophetic practice.
